        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520"><meta property="og:title" content="Lignin-Derived Mesoporous Carbon for Sodium-Ion Batteries: Block Copolymer Soft Templating and Carbon Microstructure Analysis"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/asset/images/acs.chemmater.3c01520.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="The demand for versatile and sustainable energy materials is on the rise, given the importance of developing novel clean technologies for transition to a net zero economy. Here, we present the synthesis, characterization, and application of lignin-derived ordered mesoporous carbons with various pore sizes (from 5 to approximately 50 nm) as anodes in sodium-ion batteries. We have varied the pore size using self-synthesized PEOn-b-PHAm block copolymers with different PEO and PHA chain lengths, applying the “soft templating” approach to introduce isolated spherical pores of 20 to 50 nm in diameters. The pore structure was evaluated by transmission electron microscopy (TEM), nitrogen physisorption, and small-angle X-ray scattering (SAXS). We report the microstructure analysis of such mesoporous lignin-based carbons using Raman spectroscopy and wide-angle X-ray scattering (WAXS). In comparison with nontemplated carbon and carbons templated employing commercial Pluronic F-127 and PIB50-b-PEO45, which created accessible channels and spherical pores up to approximately 10 nm in diameter, the carbon microstructure analysis revealed that templating with all applied polymers significantly impedes graphitization upon thermal treatment. Furthermore, the gained knowledge of similar carbon microstructures regardless of the type of template allowed the investigation of the influence of different pore morphologies in carbon applied as an anode material in sodium-ion batteries, supporting the previous theories in the literature that closed pores are beneficial for sodium storage while providing insights into the importance of pore size."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/asset/images/acs.chemmater.3c01520.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/nwyw~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="90f6e19c19a9cd95-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6e19c19a9cd95-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '10a13f42-4a4c-4cd1-8f93-03d076ec1243'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

type="text/x-mathjax-config;executed=true">MathJax.Hub.Config({
            menuSettings: {
                zoom: "Click",
                zscale: "150%"
            },
            SVG: {
                fonts: ["STIX-Web"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
            },
            "HTML-CSS": {
                fonts: ["STIX"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
                scale: 90,
            },
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("mml Jax Ready", function () {
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            MML.mi.prototype.setTeXclass = function (prev) {
                this.getPrevClass(prev);
                return this;
            };
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                setTeXclass: function (prev) {
                    this.getPrevClass(prev);
                    for (var i = 0, m = this.data.length; i < m; i++) {
                        if (this.data[i]) this.data[i].setTeXclass();
                    }
                    return this;
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("SVG Jax Ready", function () {
            var FONT = MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"] || MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"];
            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("MathML Jax Ready", function () {
            var PARSE = MathJax.InputJax.MathML.Parse;
            var ADD = PARSE.prototype.AddChildren;
            PARSE.Augment({
                AddChildren: function (mml, node) {
                    ADD.call(this, mml, node);
                    if (mml.type === 'mrow') {
                        var first = mml.data[0], last = mml.data[mml.data.length-1];
                        if (mml.open && !mml.data[0].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.open;
                        if (mml.close && !mml.data[mml.data.length - 1].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.close;
                    }
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS Jax Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax['HTML-CSS'];
            var DELIMITERS = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.DELIMITERS;
            var FONT = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"] || HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"]

            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;

            MML.mrow.Augment({
              _HTMLlineBreaks: MML.mrow.prototype.HTMLlineBreaks,
              HTMLlineBreaks: function (span) {

                if (this.parent.linebreakContainer && this.Get("display") === "inline") return false;
                return this._HTMLlineBreaks(span);
              }
            });


            DELIMITERS[0x0305] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x00AF};
            DELIMITERS[0x033F] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x003D};
            DELIMITERS[0x20E1] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x2194};

            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS multiline Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax["HTML-CSS"];
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            var PENALTY = MML.mbase.prototype.HTMLlinebreakPenalty;

            MML.msqrt.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mroot.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mspace.Augment({
                defaultDef: {
                    indentalign: MML.INDENTALIGN.AUTO,
                    indentshift: "0",
                    indenttarget: "",
                    indentalignfirst: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftfirst: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT,
                    indentalignlast: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftlast: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT
                },
                HTMLbetterBreak: function (info,state) {
                    if (info.values && info.values.id === this.spanID) {return false}
                    var values = this.getValues(
                        "linebreak",
                        "indentalign","indentshift",
                        "indentalignfirst","indentshiftfirst",
                        "indentalignlast","indentshiftlast"
                    );
                    var linebreakValue = values.linebreak;
                    if (!linebreakValue || this.hasDimAttr()) {
                        linebreakValue = MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO;
                    }
                    var W = info.scanW, span = this.HTMLspanElement(), w = span.bbox.w;
                    if (span.style.paddingLeft) {w += HTMLCSS.unEm(span.style.paddingLeft)}
                    if (W - info.shift === 0) {return false} // don't break at zero width
                    var offset = HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth - W;

                    if (state.n === 0 && (values.indentshiftfirst !== state.VALUES.indentshiftfirst ||
                        values.indentalignfirst !== state.VALUES.indentalignfirst)) {
                        var align = this.HTMLgetAlign(state,values),
                            shift = this.HTMLgetShift(state,values,align);
                        offset += (info.shift - shift);
                    }

                    var penalty = Math.floor(offset / HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth * 1000);
                    if (penalty < 0) {penalty = PENALTY.toobig - 3*penalty}
                    penalty += info.nest * PENALTY.nestfactor;

                    var linebreak = PENALTY[linebreakValue]||0;
                    if (linebreakValue === MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO && w >= PENALTY.spacelimit &&
                        !this.mathbackground && !this.background)
                    {linebreak = [(w+PENALTY.spaceoffset)*PENALTY.spacefactor]}
                    if (!MathJax.Object.isArray(linebreak)) {
                        if (linebreak || offset >= 0) {penalty = linebreak * info.nest}
                    } else {penalty = Math.max(1,penalty + linebreak[0] * info.nest)}

                    if (penalty >= info.penalty) {return false}
                    info.penalty = penalty; info.values = values; info.W = W; info.w = w;
                    values.lineleading = state.VALUES.lineleading;
                    values.linebreakstyle = "before"; values.id = this.spanID;
                    return true;
                }
            });


            
            PENALTY.goodbreak = [-400];

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6e19c19a9cd95-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'chemistry_materials';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/chemistry_materials/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/chemistry_materials/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

<script nonce="90f6e19c19a9cd95-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "cmatex";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Lignin-Derived Mesoporous Carbon for Sodium-Ion Batteries: Block Copolymer Soft Templating and Carbon Microstructure Analysis", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Lignin-Derived Mesoporous Carbon for Sodium-Ion Batteries: Block Copolymer Soft Templating and Carbon Microstructure Analysis", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Lignin-Derived Mesoporous Carbon for Sodium-Ion Batteries: Block Copolymer Soft Templating and Carbon Microstructure Analysis", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Lignin-Derived Mesoporous Carbon for Sodium-Ion Batteries: Block Copolymer Soft Templating and Carbon Microstructure Analysis", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Lignin-Derived Mesoporous Carbon for Sodium-Ion Batteries: Block Copolymer Soft Templating and Carbon Microstructure Analysis", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Lignin-Derived Mesoporous Carbon for Sodium-Ion Batteries: Block Copolymer Soft Templating and Carbon Microstructure Analysis", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Lignin-Derived Mesoporous Carbon for Sodium-Ion Batteries: Block Copolymer Soft Templating and Carbon Microstructure Analysis", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Lignin-Derived Mesoporous Carbon for Sodium-Ion Batteries: Block Copolymer Soft Templating and Carbon Microstructure Analysis", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Lignin-Derived Mesoporous Carbon for Sodium-Ion Batteries: Block Copolymer Soft Templating and Carbon Microstructure Analysis", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

                                <div role="tooltip" id="tooltip-cc-text-header" class="article__hover"><div class="article__tooltip" data-id="article_drawer_CC-Tooltip"><h3>License Summary*</h3><p>You are free to <span class="bold">share </span>(copy and redistribute) this article in any medium or format and to <span class="bold">adapt </span>(remix, transform, and build upon) the material for any purpose, even commercially within the parameters below:</p><ul><li><img src="/specs/products/achs/releasedAssets/images/cc-license/cc.svg" alt="cc licence" width="22" height="22"><p>Creative Commons (CC): This is a Creative Commons license.</p></li><li><img src="/specs/products/achs/releasedAssets/images/cc-license/by.svg" alt="ny licence" width="22" height="22"><p>Attribution (BY): Credit must be given to the creator.</p></li></ul><a href="https://creativecommons.org/licenses/by/4.0/" class="bold" data-id="article_drawer_CC-License_Tooltip_Link">View full license</a><div class="disclaimer"><p class="bold">*Disclaimer</p><p>This summary highlights only some of the key features and terms of the

                                            </p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_header-metrics-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="article_header-metrics-dropzone"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/asset/images/medium/cm3c01520_0013.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/asset/images/large/cm3c01520_0013.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">The demand for versatile and sustainable energy materials is on the rise, given the importance of developing novel clean technologies for transition to a net zero economy. Here, we present the synthesis, characterization, and application of lignin-derived ordered mesoporous carbons with various pore sizes (from 5 to approximately 50 nm) as anodes in sodium-ion batteries. We have varied the pore size using self-synthesized PEO<sub><i>n</i></sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub><i>m</i></sub> block copolymers with different PEO and PHA chain lengths, applying the “soft templating” approach to introduce isolated spherical pores of 20 to 50 nm in diameters. The pore structure was evaluated by transmission electron microscopy (TEM), nitrogen physisorption, and small-angle X-ray scattering (SAXS). We report the microstructure analysis of such mesoporous lignin-based carbons using Raman spectroscopy and wide-angle X-ray scattering (WAXS). In comparison with nontemplated carbon and carbons templated employing commercial Pluronic F-127 and PIB<sub>50</sub>-<i>b</i>-PEO<sub>45</sub>, which created accessible channels and spherical pores up to approximately 10 nm in diameter, the carbon microstructure analysis revealed that templating with all applied polymers significantly impedes graphitization upon thermal treatment. Furthermore, the gained knowledge of similar carbon microstructures regardless of the type of template allowed the investigation of the influence of different pore morphologies in carbon applied as an anode material in sodium-ion batteries, supporting the previous theories in the literature that closed pores are beneficial for sodium storage while providing insights into the importance of pore size.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under</p><div class="article__cc-license__inter"><div class="article__cc-license--hoverable"><a href="https://creativecommons.org/licenses/by/4.0/" aria-describedby="tooltip-cc-text" class="link--bold" data-id="article_abstract_CC-License">CC-BY 4.0
                                    <span class="arrow"></span></a>
                                .

Published by American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291243&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Copolymers" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Copolymers" class="article__tags__link">Copolymers</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291439&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Genetics" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Genetics" class="article__tags__link">Genetics</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292475&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Polymers" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Polymers" class="article__tags__link">Polymers</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292275&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Transmission electron microscopy" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Transmission%20electron%20microscopy" class="article__tags__link">Transmission electron microscopy</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291786&amp;ref=fulltext" title="X-ray scattering" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_X-ray%20scattering" class="article__tags__link">X-ray scattering</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div class="extra-info-sec articleNote"><h4><i class="icon-note-flag" aria-hidden="true"></i>Special Issue</h4><p class="last">Published as part of <i>Chemistry of Materials</i> <a href="/page/virtual-collections.html?journal=cmatex&amp;ref=feature" class="ext-link">virtual special issue</a> “In Honor of Prof. Clement Sanchez”.</p></div><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Soft templating using amphiphilic polymers is a simple and suitable synthetic approach toward the production of mesoporous carbon materials, as initiated with the studies by Dai and co-workers. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1" aria-label="reference 1">(1)</a> Properties such as a high specific surface area, good chemical and mechanical resistance, and a tunable pore structure enable a wide scope of different applications as in catalysis, adsorption, or energy storage, for instance. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2 ref3 ref4'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref2 ref3 ref4" aria-label="reference 2 to 4">(2−4)</a> The evaporation-induced self-assembly (EISA) process is often applied to create an ordered mesophase of templates and precursors. Regarding this process, Clément Sanchez has brought significant scientific contributions to porous materials, especially by performing insightful mechanistic studies to reveal the underlying sol–gel chemistry of cross-linkable structures. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5 ref6'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 5 and 6">(5,6)</a> Next to the feasible production of porous carbons, the importance of choosing sustainable bioderived and widely available precursors is key in the quest to produce sustainable energy materials. For example, Ghimbeu et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7" aria-label="reference 7">(7)</a> substituted the carcinogen formaldehyde as a cross-linking agent in conventional synthetic strategies with phenol, resorcinol, or phloroglucinol as carbon precursors. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8" aria-label="reference 8">(8)</a> Furthermore, Herou et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9" aria-label="reference 9">(9)</a> used “organosolv lignin” as a bioprecursor to replace the fossil fuel derivate phloroglucinol in the synthesis of mesoporous carbon templated with Pluronic F-127. Applying lignin as a precursor exhibits obvious benefits, namely, high carbon content and large availability. Despite being the second most abundant natural polymer next to cellulose, lignin is rarely used in high-value applications such as the synthesis of advanced energy materials, for instance. It usually ends up as a waste product in the paper industry after the extraction of cellulose and is usually incinerated. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10 ref11'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref11" aria-label="reference 10 and 11">(10,11)</a> Hence, the effective transformation of lignin to porous carbon materials, <i>e.g</i>., via templating approaches, is of growing interest. In most studies, soft templating approaches rely on templates that create relatively small mesopores rarely exceeding 10 nm in diameter. Apart from these, purposefully selected and synthesized block copolymers enable the generation of other pore geometries and sizes up to 40 nm. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12" aria-label="reference 12">(12)</a> An increased mesopore diameter can be crucial for applications relying on enhanced mass transport, which is otherwise diffusion-limited within micropores or small mesopores. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> Although research on various block copolymers as soft templates has been carried out for several years, their utilization in large-scale applications is still hindered by often complex and time-consuming synthetic processes. Recent studies by Wagner et al., <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> as well as the initial work by Lokupitiya et al., <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14" aria-label="reference 14">(14)</a> revealed that PEO<sub><i>n</i></sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub><i>m</i></sub> (poly(ethylene oxide)<sub><i>n</i></sub>-<i>b</i>-poly(<i>n</i>-hexyl acrylate)<sub><i>m</i></sub> with <i>n</i> and <i>m</i> as numbers of the respective repeating units) block copolymers can be suitable templates for various metal oxides creating large mesopores, which is a challenge to be introduced by soft templating without additional pore size swelling agents. Their synthesis via a supplemental activator reducing agent atom transfer radical polymerization (SARA ATRP) enables facile adjustment of different block chain lengths and is feasible with standard laboratory equipment, which represents a major advantage. In this study, the applicability of PEO<sub><i>n</i></sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub><i>m</i></sub> block copolymers as templates for lignin-derived carbons with different pore diameters due to varying block lengths is evaluated. As previous investigations are restricted to PEO<sub><i>n</i></sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub><i>m</i></sub> templating of metal oxides, here we extend the versatile application of PEO<sub><i>n</i></sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub><i>m</i></sub> block copolymers also for mesoporous carbon next to the templating of metal oxides.</div><div class="NLM_p">Since reduced solubility comes with an increased molecular weight of polymers, which is necessary to produce large pore diameters, precipitation or phase separation would obstruct the EISA mechanism. Moreover, the thermodynamic and kinetic stability of block copolymer micelles formed within the EISA process is necessary to resist polymerization reactions during heat-induced precursor cross-linking. Hence, a rational choice of the solvent and reaction conditions becomes a crucial challenge when large block copolymers are used as templates in contrast to the straightforward application of commercial templates from the Pluronic family. Additionally, as lignin exhibits a highly branched molecular structure and high polydispersity, a uniform arrangement around large self-arranged polymer micelles during the EISA process is key. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> To explore the general possibility of introducing large mesopores in carbons based on a precursor mixture of phloroglucinol and organosolv lignin in a mass ratio of 1:1, PEO<sub><i>n</i></sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub><i>m</i></sub> block copolymers were employed in this study. Further, the comparison with two additional types of block copolymers (Pluronic F-127 and PIB<sub>50</sub>-<i>b</i>-PEO<sub>45</sub>) as templates, all applied in a mass ratio of 1:1 between the template and carbon precursors, aims to investigate the difference in accessibility, shape, and size of generated pores due to different soft templates for application as an anode material in sodium-ion batteries.</div><div class="NLM_p">In addition to investigating the pore morphology based on the type of block copolymer, the effect of the block copolymer template on carbonization and graphitization behavior is also evaluated. The nongraphitizable character and microstructure of conventional nontemplated resin-based carbons have been well investigated already. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16 ref17 ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17 ref18" aria-label="reference 16 to 18">(16−18)</a> The absence of crystalline long-range order within the graphene layer stacking and structural disorder of the two-dimensional graphene lattice cause broad and asymmetric (<i>hk</i>) and (00<i>l</i>) reflections in the wide-angle X-ray scattering (WAXS) pattern instead of general (<i>hkl</i>) reflections. Hence, conventional line-width-based microstructure evaluation of nongraphitic carbons is hindered and not meaningful. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18 ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 18 and 19">(18,19)</a> Therefore, applying the algorithm by Ruland and Smarsly <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> has proved itself as a useful tool for nongraphitic carbon microstructure analysis in previous studies. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16 ref18 ref19 ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref18 ref19 ref21" aria-label="reference 16, 18, 19 and 21">(16,18,19,21)</a> However, due to the novelty of the synthetic approach of producing carbons from organosolv lignin, the development and structural features of the microstructure of lignin-derived carbons have not been investigated yet. Inspired by Clément Sanchez and his pursuit of a detailed analysis and materials characterization, this study aims to present a starting point for enlightening the microstructure of templated lignin-derived hard carbons. This provides an initial understanding of the carbonization behavior of lignin isolated in the organosolv process. Also, using varying block copolymers as templates compared to nontemplated lignin-based carbon enables us to investigate the impact of different templates on carbonization behavior. This offers a door opener for follow-up studies of carbon analysis of different synthesis and precursor systems.</div><div class="NLM_p last">The resulting carbon materials from this study with different pore sizes, pore accessibility, and graphitization levels were applied as anode materials in sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), the next generation of commercial and more sustainable batteries to complement lithium-ion batteries. Hard carbons, as, <i>e.g</i>., our synthesized mesoporous lignin-based carbon materials are the most utilized types of carbons for anodes in SIBs. However, their sodium storage mechanism is still under debate. The latest theories reported in the literature support a combination of adsorption–desorption of Na<sup>+</sup> at defects and intercalation in the sloping region of the charge/discharge curve and a pore-filling mechanism in the plateau region, which needs closed pores. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22 ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref23" aria-label="reference 22 and 23">(22,23)</a> Based on these theories, to maximize sodium storage, an interlayer spacing above 3.5 to 3.6 Å and closed pores are necessary. Since the determination of the exact value of this important structural parameter from WAXS data is impeded by the translational disorder in the stacking, the fitting approach by Ruland and Smarsly <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> was applied to obtain as reliable values as possible, which we compared to WAXS standard analysis (Scherrer-type analysis). The larger the pore dimension, the higher the plateau capacity should be, provided that the interlayer spacing stays within the mentioned range. For carbons with open porosity, the Coulombic efficiency should be low due to extensive electrolyte decomposition on the high surface area provided by open pores. As for formality, it should be noted that the terminology of “closed pores” will be continued instead of “voids”, as accessibility for sodium ions is still given here. Applying PEO<sub><i>n</i></sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub><i>m</i></sub> polymers as templates for these lignin-derived hard carbons with both open and closed pores and a similar carbonization degree leading to a rather similar graphitic microstructure validates this previous sodium storage mechanism. These are just preliminary results since the focus here was the synthesis and characterization of this new type of porous lignin-derived carbons, but the ability to control closed vs open pores, their pore size, and pore volume opens new avenues for a better validation of the sodium-ion storage mechanism in hard carbons.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i4">Experimental Methods</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> Materials</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Phloroglucinol (1,3,5-benzentriol, ≥99.0%), the triblock copolymer Pluronic F-127 (poly(ethylene oxide)<sub><i>x</i></sub>-poly(propylene oxide)<sub><i>y</i></sub>-poly(ethylene oxide)<sub><i>x</i></sub>, <i>M</i><sub>W</sub> = 12.6 kDa), and methanol (≥99.8%) were obtained from <i>Sigma-Aldrich</i>. Aqueous glyoxal solution (40 wt %) was purchased from <i>Alfa Aesar</i>. Chemicals were used as received without any further treatment. Organosolv lignin extracted from beech wood was provided by the Fraunhofer Center for Chemical-Biotechnological Processes (CBP) in Jena. Further information on lignin characterization and extraction can be found elsewhere. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> The diblock copolymer PIB<sub>50</sub>-<i>b</i>-PEO<sub>45</sub> (poly(isobutylene)-<i>block</i>-poly(ethylene oxide)) was purchased from <i>BASF</i> (<i>Ludwigshafen, Germany</i>), while the diblock copolymers PEO<sub><i>n</i></sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub><i>m</i></sub> (poly(ethylene oxide)-<i>b</i>-poly(<i>n</i>-hexyl acrylate)) were synthesized as described below.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> Synthesis of PEO<sub><i>n</i></sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub><i>m</i></sub> Diblock Copolymers</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The two-step synthesis was originally reported by Lokupitiya et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14" aria-label="reference 14">(14)</a> According to Wagner et al., <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> PEO-Br as a halide-capped macroinitiator was synthesized by esterification of α-methyl-ω-hydroxy poly(ethylene oxide). In a subsequent supplemental activator reducing agent atom transfer radical polymerization (SARA ATRP), PEO<sub><i>n</i></sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub><i>m</i></sub> copolymers of various block lengths were obtained. Details on PEO<sub><i>n</i></sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub><i>m</i></sub> diblock copolymer synthesis can be found in the recent literature. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/suppl_file/cm3c01520_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a> (SI) provides an overview of the used amounts of starting materials to synthesize respective polymers (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/suppl_file/cm3c01520_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>) and polymer characterization by <sup>1</sup>H NMR (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/suppl_file/cm3c01520_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S1–S6</a>), gel permeation chromatography (GPC) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/suppl_file/cm3c01520_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>), and dynamic light scattering (DLS) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/suppl_file/cm3c01520_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>).</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7"> Preparation of Carbon Materials</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The procedure in the initial report of lignin-derived mesoporous carbons by Herou et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9" aria-label="reference 9">(9)</a> has been adjusted as follows: The synthesis of the mesoporous carbon materials was carried out by applying an EISA process. First, the respective block copolymer (2.25 g) was dissolved in methanol by ultrasonication (45 kHz, 70 °C). For Pluronic F-127 and PIB<sub>50</sub>-<i>b</i>-PEO<sub>45</sub> as templates, polymer dispersions were obtained in 62.5 mL of methanol in a glass beaker. Higher molecular mass causing poorer solubility required doubling the volume of solvent (125 mL of methanol) to dissolve the PEO<sub><i>n</i></sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub><i>m</i></sub> diblock copolymers. One after another, the carbon precursors, lignin (0.65 g) and phloroglucinol (0.65 g), and aqueous glyoxal solution (1.25 mL) as a cross-linker were added after the complete dissolution of each by ultrasonication. In the case of PEO<sub>214</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>322</sub> polymer dispersion, an additional 30 mL of methanol was necessary to dissolve lignin completely. Covering the beakers prevented undesired solvent evaporation during ultrasonic treatment. Afterward, the solutions were left open in order to evaporate methanol at 40 °C for one night. Shiny, hard, and brown solids were obtained in each beaker. The following thermopolymerization was initiated upon thermal treatment at 85 °C overnight. The solids obtained were transferred to crucibles. Template removal at 350 °C and carbonization at 900 °C under an inert atmosphere (<i>STF 16/180</i> from <i>Carbolite Ltd.</i>; N<sub>2</sub> flow rate of 500 mL min<sup>–1</sup>; heating rate of 1 °C min<sup>–1</sup>; 1 h dwelling time at 350 °C; further temperature increase with 1 °C min<sup>–1</sup>; 2 h dwelling time at 900 °C, natural cooling) yielded the respective carbon material as the product. Reactant mass ratios of carbon precursor: template: aqueous glyoxal and phloroglucinol: lignin were chosen according to the previous literature <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9" aria-label="reference 9">(9)</a> and set to 1:1.731:1.216 and 1:1, respectively. To investigate the influence of carbonization temperature on carbon microstructure and porosity, carbonization at 1300 °C (<i>STF 16/180</i> from <i>Carbolite Ltd.</i>; N<sub>2</sub> flow rate of 500 mL min<sup>–1</sup>; heating rate of 5 °C min<sup>–1</sup>; 2 h dwelling time; natural cooling) was carried out as post-treatment.</div></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8"> Preparation of Carbon Anodes</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The carbon materials were ground using a mortar and pestle to break down the monolithic structure and homogenize the particle size. After that, the carbon powder was mixed with the binder carboxymethyl cellulose (<i>Sigma-Aldrich</i>) in a mass ratio of 9:1 using a mortar and pestle for 30 min to prepare an aqueous slurry. The hard carbon electrodes were then coated by using the mixed slurry onto Al foil (<i>MTI Corporation</i>). Before being cut into pieces for the further assembly of coin cells, the coated Al foil was dried at room temperature, followed by 80 °C in a vacuum oven overnight. The loading mass of the coated electrodes (0.8 cm × 0.8 cm) was controlled within a range of 1.5–2.0 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>.</div></div><div id="sec2_5" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9"> Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The electrochemical testing was conducted using stainless steel coin cells (CR2032, <i>MTI Corporation</i>) at room temperature, and the cell assembly was completed in an Ar-filled glovebox (H<sub>2</sub>O, O<sub>2</sub> &lt; 0.5 ppm, <i>mBraun</i>). The commercial electrolytes of 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in mixed solvents, ethylene carbonate–diethyl carbonate (EC–DMC, 1:1 vol %, <i>Elyte</i>), were used. In the half-cell configuration, a piece of sodium metal (12 mm in diameter, <i>Alfa Aesar</i>) was used as both the counter and the reference electrodes. The electrodes were placed against the sodium metal with a glass fiber separator (16 mm in diameter, GF/A Glass microfiber filters, <i>Whatman</i>) soaked with 100 μL of electrolytes. The half cells were discharged and charged between 0.001 and 2.5 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na at different current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, and 2.0 C (1 C = 300 mA g<sup>–1</sup>) as well as cycling at 0.5 C.</div></div><div id="sec2_6" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i10"> Galvanostatic Intermittent Titration Technique (GITT)</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Before the GITT measurement, the samples were cycled at a constant current of 0.1 C for the first cycle. This precycle was to exclude the influence of SEI formation. From the second cycle, a sample was galvanostatically discharged for 10 min and then rested for 80 min until the potential reached 1 mV (V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na). The discharge current was based on the reversible capacity to ensure similar numbers of data points across different samples. For example, the reversible capacities of the PIB<sub>50</sub>-<i>b</i>-PEO<sub>45</sub>-templated carbon and the PEO<sub>428</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>265</sub>-templated carbon are around 100 and 220 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Then, the discharge currents were 10 and 22 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. According to the potential gap between equilibrium potential and nonequilibrium potential under applied current, the diffusivity coefficient can be estimated using Fick’s second law with the following simplified equation <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="ueq1"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math MJXp-display" id="MJXp-Span-1"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-2"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-37" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0em; vertical-align: -0.689em;"><span class="MJXp-right MJXp-scale4" style="font-size: 3.756em; margin-left: -0.17em;">(</span></span><span class="MJXp-mfrac" id="MJXp-Span-38" style="vertical-align: 0.25em;"><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-39"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-40">Δ</span><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-41"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-42" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">E</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-43" class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-46"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-47" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">E</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-48" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;">t</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-49" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0em; vertical-align: -0.689em;"><span class="MJXp-right MJXp-scale4" style="font-size: 3.756em; margin-left: -0.17em;">)</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mn MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-50" style="vertical-align: 1.54em;">2</span></span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-1"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>D</mi></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mfrac><mn>4</mn><mrow><mi>π</mi><mo lspace="0.03em" rspace="0.03em">·</mo><mi>τ</mi></mrow></mfrac><msup><mrow><mo stretchy="true">(</mo><mfrac><mrow><msub><mi mathvariant="italic">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">B</mi></msub><mo lspace="0.03em" rspace="0.03em">·</mo><msub><mi>V</mi><mi mathvariant="italic">m</mi></msub></mrow><mrow><msub><mi>M</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">B</mi></msub><mo lspace="0.03em" rspace="0.03em">·</mo><mi mathvariant="italic">S</mi></mrow></mfrac><mo stretchy="true">)</mo></mrow><mn>2</mn></msup><msup><mrow><mo stretchy="true">(</mo><mfrac><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi><msub><mi>E</mi><mi mathvariant="italic">S</mi></msub></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi><msub><mi>E</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">t</mi></msub></mrow></mfrac><mo stretchy="true">)</mo></mrow><mn>2</mn></msup></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"></div></div>where τ is pulse time length, <i>m</i><sub>B</sub>, <i>M</i><sub>B</sub>, and <i>V</i><sub>M</sub> are the active mass, the molar mass, and the molar volume of carbon, respectively, and <i>S</i> is the active area of the working electrode (in this work, the area of the electrode was used in the calculation), Δ<i>E</i><sub>S</sub> and Δ<i>E<sub>t</sub></i> can be obtained from a GITT curve (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/suppl_file/cm3c01520_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a> in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/suppl_file/cm3c01520_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI</a> for a representative curve in GITT).</div></div><div id="sec2_7" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i12" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i12"> Characterization of PEO<sub><i>n</i></sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub><i>m</i></sub> Polymers</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The hydrodynamic diameter was determined by DLS experiments carried out on a <i>Litesizer 500</i> from <i>Anton Paar</i>. Polymer dispersions used in the synthesis procedure were diluted 4-fold in a disposable measurement cell. Measurement was performed in three runs of ten seconds each run and evaluated with the software <i>Anton Paar Kalliope Professional 2.18.1</i>. Proton-nuclear magnetic resonance (<sup>1</sup>H NMR) experiments were performed at 25 °C on a <i>Bruker Avance II 400 MHz</i>, <i>Bruker Avance III 400 MHz HD</i>, and <i>Bruker Ascend Advance IV Neo 7</i>. The spectra were referenced to the solvent peak at 7.27 ppm and evaluated using <i>MestReNova 14.1.2</i>. Tetrahydrofuran with a flow rate of 0.5 mL min<sup>–1</sup> served as an eluent for GPC. Detection with simultaneous UV (<i>TSP UV 2000</i>) and refractive index (<i>Shodex RI-101</i>) took place at 25 °C. Calibration was done with poly(styrene) standards (<i>PSS, Mainz, Germany</i>). A 300 mm × 8 mm <i>PSS SDV linear M</i> column was applied as a stationary phase and packed with 3 μm particles with 10<sup>2</sup> to 10<sup>6</sup> Da of mass range. An injection volume of 100 μL was filtered through 0.45 μm filters beforehand and contained about 0.15 wt % polymeric sample.</div></div><div id="sec2_8" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i13" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i13"> Characterization of Carbon Materials</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Nitrogen physisorption experiments were performed with <i>TriStar II PLUS</i> from <i>Micromeritics</i> at 77 K. Data evaluation was carried out with the affiliated software <i>TriStar II PLUS 3.00</i>. For pore size distribution determination, a nonlocal density functional theory (NLDFT) kernel for carbon with cylindrical pore geometry was applied to the adsorption branch. Degassing of the carbon samples was accomplished at 220 °C for 16 h with <i>Micromeritics′s Smart VacPrep</i> and the <i>Smart VacPrep</i> software. Transmission electron microscopy (TEM) was carried out for the direct imaging of carbon materials in a <i>JEOL JEM-2100F</i> at 200 kV, and the images were analyzed with <i>DigitalMicrograph</i> 3.52.4932.0 from <i>Gatan</i>. The carbon powders were dispersed in acetone by sonicating for 15 min and left to settle for 10 min. The upper solution was collected and deposited onto the TEM grids (Holey Carbon Films on 300 Mesh Copper Grids, <i>Agar Scientific</i>). The grid was then left to dry overnight and stored in dry conditions before imaging. A Scanning Electron Microscope (<i>LEO Gemini 1252 FEG-SEM</i>) was used to study the morphologies of samples operating at an accelerating voltage of 3 keV and a working distance of around 3 mm. Before the measurement, the samples were secured onto the alumina sample holders by using conductive double-sided carbon tapes. Wide-angle X-ray scattering (WAXS) measurements were performed with an <i>X′Pert Pro MPD</i> diffractometer from <i>PANalytical</i> in Bragg–Brentano geometry at room temperature. Cu-<i>K</i><sub>α1</sub> radiation (λ = 1.5406 Å) and Cu-<i>K</i><sub>α2</sub> radiation (λ = 1.5444 Å) in a 1:1 ratio at 40 kV and 40 mA were applied. Measurements were operated with a step size of 0.08° in the range of 5° &lt; 2θ &lt; 115°. Data analysis was based on the algorithm of Ruland and Smarsly <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> implemented in the <i>OctCarb</i> script by Osswald and Smarsly <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> and executed by the GNU <i>Octave</i> software. Details about the mathematical evaluation of quantitative microstructure parameters from the whole WAXS pattern of nongraphitic carbons can be found elsewhere. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18 ref19 ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19 ref20" aria-label="reference 18 to 20">(18−20)</a> Small-angle X-ray scattering (SAXS) was operated with a <i>SAXSpoint 2.0</i> instrument from <i>Anton Paar</i> equipped with a <i>Dectris EIGER2 R 1M</i> hybrid pixel area X-ray detector and a microsource creating point-focused and slit-collimated Cu–K<sub>α1</sub> (λ = 1.54 Å) radiation at 50 W. The sample holder from <i>Anton Paar</i>, on which the sample plate was fixed with a vacuum-tight sealing tape at both sides, at a motorized X/Y table consisted of a 0.4 nm thick metal plate with 20 square holes (4 mm × 4 mm with 10 mm center-to-center distances). Sample-to-detector distance was set to 575.65 mm. Measurement was carried out with 25 runs for each sample in a vacuum (1 mbar) and at 25 °C temperature. Each run contained an exposure time of 2 min. On the one hand, SAXS data evaluation was carried out with chord length distribution (CLD) analysis according to Stoeckel et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> On the other hand, the software <i>SASfit</i> 0.94.12 was applied to fit the SAXS patterns, in which the implemented decoupling approximation described the fitting model with a lattice parameter and a form factor for spheres with a Gaussian size distribution. In addition, modeling SAXS patterns assuming a face-centered cubic (FCC) array of polydisperse spheres was accomplished with the software <i>Gnuplot</i>. Applying a laser excitation wavelength of λ = 532 nm and a power of 6.25 mW, Raman spectra were recorded with a <i>Senterra II</i> instrument by <i>Bruker</i>. Data export was carried out with <i>Opus 8.1</i>. Density measurements were carried out with a <i>Micromeritics AccuPyc II 1340 Gas Pycnometer</i>, which was operated with the software <i>AccuPyc II 1340 V2.00</i>. A 1 mL volume cuvette for the analyzed sample, 25 °C setting temperature, and helium as working gas were used. The procedure consisted of 20 purges over 50 cycles. The results for the last 30 cycles were applied to obtain an average density value.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i14">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The synthesis of mesoporous carbons was carried out by soft templating with fixed mass ratios of template, carbon precursors, and aqueous glyoxal solution (1.731:1:1.216) as well as within the carbon precursors phloroglucinol and organosolv lignin (1:1). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9" aria-label="reference 9">(9)</a> While all other synthetic conditions were maintained identically, different block copolymers were applied as templates to test their applicability for soft templating of lignin-based carbons and to achieve mesoporosity with controlled pore size and shape. Next to the well-established triblock copolymer Pluronic F-127, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9 ref27 ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref27 ref28" aria-label="reference 9, 27 and 28">(9,27,28)</a> the diblock copolymer PIB<sub>50</sub>-<i>b</i>-PEO<sub>45</sub> (poly(isobutylene)-<i>block</i>-poly(ethylene)) and three PEO<sub><i>n</i></sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub><i>m</i></sub>-type (poly(ethylene oxide)-<i>b</i>-poly(<i>n</i>-hexyl acrylate)) polymers (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>A) with varying block lengths (both <i>n</i> and <i>m</i>) are applied as templates within the synthetic procedure, which is schematically illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>, yielding mesoporous carbons.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/asset/images/medium/cm3c01520_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/asset/images/large/cm3c01520_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Schematic synthetic procedure at macroscopic and molecular level. (A) Molecular structure of PEO<sub><i>n</i></sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub><i>m</i></sub> block copolymers. (B) Schematic representation of initial precursor dispersion with fixed mass ratios of template, carbon precursors, and aqueous glyoxal solution (1.731:1:1.216) and (C) molecular assembly due to hydrogen bonding interaction. Furthermore, (D) ordered mesophase after the EISA process and thermopolymerization with (E) the resulting covalent bonding. (F) Mesoporous carbon after carbonization at 900 °C, respectively 1300 °C, in a nitrogen atmosphere.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/asset/images/large/cm3c01520_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">After the dissolution of the polymer template in methanol, organosolv lignin, phloroglucinol, and glyoxal are subsequently added to the polymer dispersion (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>B). Due to hydrogen bonding, carbon precursors possessing a hydroxy moiety interact with the PEO sequence of the templates, as indicated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>C, taking the example of phloroglucinol as a precursor. These noncovalent interactions govern the subsequent organic–organic self-assembly process. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8" aria-label="reference 8">(8)</a> Driven by the mutual incompatibility of hydrophilic and hydrophobic polymer blocks, the polymers form micelles with the hydrophobic sequence (e.g., PPO, PIB, or PHA), building the core of a micelle in methanol as a hydrophilic solvent. Note that PIB-<i>b</i>-PEO-type and PEO-<i>b</i>-PHA-type block copolymers are present as micelles already at minute concentrations, while in the case of Pluronic F-127, micelles only form at elevated concentrations upon solvent evaporation. For all three types of block copolymers, microphase separation is promoted within the EISA process, and thermopolymerization of precursor monomers at 85 °C stabilizes the ordered mesophase (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>D) by cross-linking reactions with glyoxal, thereby generating covalent carbon-bridged phenols (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>E). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27 ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref29" aria-label="reference 27 and 29">(27,29)</a> The three-dimensionally interconnected network enables subsequent template removal without collapsing mesostructures, and carbonization at 900 °C, respectively 1300 °C, yields the final mesoporous carbon (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>F). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">In order to relate the finally obtained mesopore dimensions to the block copolymer micelles, solutions of the block copolymers in methanol were studied in dynamic light scattering (DLS) measurements. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/suppl_file/cm3c01520_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>, for PIB<sub>50</sub>-<i>b</i>-PEO<sub>45</sub> and PEO<sub><i>n</i></sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub><i>m</i></sub> polymers, colloidal objects with well-defined and quite large hydrodynamic radii are observed (see <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table 1</a>), which can be attributed to micelles, in accordance with the DLS studies of Sarkar et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> It is important to note that for these two types of block copolymers, the minimized exchange dynamics of persistent micelle templates with preserved micelle dimensions allow control of yielded templated materials and defined mesoporosity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30 ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 30 and 31">(30,31)</a> In addition, the diameters increase with extended block lengths, which is in accordance with the general principles of micelle formation. As a result, the chosen combination of methanol as a solvent and PEO<sub><i>n</i></sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub><i>m</i></sub> block copolymers enables control of defined mesoporosity by micelle templating.</div><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl1"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 1. Comparison of Hydrodynamic Micelle Diameters and Pore Size in Resulting Mesoporous Carbon</div></div></div><div class="scrollable-table-wrap" style="display: block;"><table class="table
