align="left">&nbsp;</td></tr></tbody></table></div><div class="NLM_table-wrap-foot" id="IDtable-wrap-foot-t1fn1"><div class="footnote" id="t1fn1"><sup><sup>a</sup></sup><p class="last">Obtained by averaging TEM measurements.</p></div><div class="footnote" id="t1fn2"><sup><sup>b</sup></sup><p class="last">Pore diameter evaluation of carbons templated with PEO<sub><i>n</i></sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub><i>m</i></sub> polymers is not applicable, as explained later (see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>B).</p></div><div class="footnote" id="t1fn3"><sup><sup>c</sup></sup><p class="last">Obtained by fitting procedure applying a Percus–Yevick decoupling approach. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a></p></div></div></div><div class="NLM_p">Accordingly, TEM micrographs (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>) reveal the successful generation of mesoporous lignin-derived carbon by soft templating using three types of block copolymers. Lignin-based carbon templated with Pluronic F-127 exhibited characteristic channel pores (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>B) as previously produced by Herou et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9" aria-label="reference 9">(9)</a></div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/asset/images/medium/cm3c01520_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/asset/images/large/cm3c01520_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. TEM images display yielded carbons after carbonization at 900 °C synthesized (A) without any template, (B) with Pluronic F-127, and (C) with PIB<sub>50</sub>-<i>b</i>-PEO<sub>45</sub> as a template. In addition, PEO<sub><i>n</i></sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub><i>m</i></sub> polymers with varying block lengths (D) <i>n</i> = 251 and <i>m</i> = 95, (E) <i>n</i> = 428 and <i>m</i> = 265, and (F) <i>n</i> = 214 and <i>m</i> = 322 served as templates.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/asset/images/large/cm3c01520_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">PIB<sub>50</sub>-<i>b</i>-PEO<sub>45</sub> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>C) and PEO<sub><i>n</i></sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub><i>m</i></sub> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>D–F) induced arrays of spherical pores with average diameters of 9 nm (PIB<sub>50</sub>-<i>b</i>-PEO<sub>45</sub>) and 23 to 52 nm (PEO<sub><i>n</i></sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub><i>m</i></sub>). For the latter, the pore diameters increased with growing polymer block lengths and are thus in relation to the hydrodynamic micellar diameters (<a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table 1</a><b>)</b>. Hence, for PEO<sub><i>n</i></sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub><i>m</i></sub> polymers, the PHA block determined predominantly the resulting pore diameter, comparing PEO<sub>251</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>95</sub> and PEO<sub>214</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>322</sub>, featuring similar PEO blocks. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> As the determination of the hydrodynamic diameter encompasses the micelle core, the micelle corona, and a solvation sphere, it is reasonable to expect systematically smaller resulting pore sizes. These sizes predominantly depend on the micelle core. This expectation is particularly applicable when considering PIB50-b-PEO45 and PEOn-b-PHAm polymers as templates. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> The diameter of mesopores for Pluronic F-127 templated carbon is in line with the comparably small hydrodynamic radius.</div><div class="NLM_p">Carbons synthesized without any template do not reveal any mesoscale porosity, thus proving that the mesoporosity is a consequence of block polymer soft templating (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>A). Comparing PEO<sub>428</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>265</sub> and PEO<sub>251</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>95</sub>, the former results not only in larger defined spherical mesopores with (37 ± 6) nm as pore diameter but also an increased order (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>E). Such a difference in the mesoscopic ordering might be a consequence of both the block length and the polymer-to-precursor ratio, the origin of which will be addressed in a separate study varying the template concentrations. The ordered pore structures, together with uniform pore diameters visible in TEM micrographs, implement important mechanistic insight. This uniformity is possible only as polymer degradation and carbonization of the carbon matrix are decoupled. Cross-linking of the lignin-containing matrix at moderate temperatures yields a loose carbon framework through which removal of template degradation products in applied nitrogen gas flow is still possible.</div><div class="NLM_p">Yet, further extension of the PHA block length yielded variable ellipsoidal pore shapes and a larger variation in diameters (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>F). In general, as shown in <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table 1</a>, the larger the block copolymer chain lengths, the broader the distribution of hydrodynamic micelle diameters and pore sizes subsequently. Simultaneously to growing pore diameters, pore wall thicknesses of carbons with spherical pores were extended (<a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table 1</a>). Evaluation of the SEM micrographs confirmed the trends determined by TEM regarding the pore size in relation to the respective block copolymers (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/suppl_file/cm3c01520_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>). Notably, SEM images of the templated carbons display well-defined mesopores throughout the monolithic materials’ entirety, especially the absence of dense nontemplated carbon (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/suppl_file/cm3c01520_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>).</div><div class="NLM_p">To further gain knowledge of the pore morphology introduced by soft templating, nitrogen physisorption experiments (<i>T</i> = 77 K) were conducted (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>). The lack of porosity in the nontemplated carbon sample, as indicated in the physisorption isotherm (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>A) and pore size distribution (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>B), agrees with TEM observations. Taking a closer look at the polymer-templated carbons, templating with Pluronic F-127 and PIB<sub>50</sub>-<i>b</i>-PEO<sub>45</sub> led to isotherms with H2-type-shaped hysteresis loops <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> and a pronounced nitrogen uptake (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>A), indicating significant mesopore volume. To evaluate the desorption mechanism and whether pore blocking or cavitation network effect might cause delayed evaporation, especially for PIB<sub>50</sub>-<i>b</i>-PEO<sub>45</sub>-templated carbon, further investigation by varying the adsorptive or recording a series of hysteresis scans is necessary, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> which is not in the focus of the present study. Nevertheless, the occurrence of hysteresis is associated with restricted access to the pore cavity. The pore size distributions in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>B were determined by applying an NLDFT kernel for carbon with a cylindrical mesopore geometry on the adsorption branch. Instead, using an NLDFT analysis assuming a spherical geometry for the large mesopores is expected to yield up to 50% larger pore diameters. These would present an even larger discrepancy with observed TEM results, which might be caused by the assumption of graphitic carbon with different polarities compared to the present nongraphitic, hard carbon implemented in the NLDFT kernel. Hence, an NLDFT kernel for cylindrical mesoporous carbon is applied, though being aware of pore diameters being slightly larger. It reveals global maxima at 6 nm (Pluronic F-127), respectively 11 nm (PIB<sub>50</sub>-<i>b</i>-PEO<sub>45</sub>), i.e., slightly larger pore diameters compared to mean pore diameters determined by averaged TEM measurements. Physisorption experiments of the carbon templated with PEO<sub><i>n</i></sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub><i>m</i></sub> polymers show different results. They display characteristic carbon microporosity with initial nitrogen uptake in the isotherm at a small relative pressure in all templated carbon materials. Yet, despite the abundance of mesopores displayed in TEM and SEM images and also contrary to SAXS analysis (see below), quite surprisingly, the overall pore volume is comparably small and hysteresis loops are absent in recorded isotherms (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>A), thereby suggesting the presence of micropores and small mesopores as dominant pore populations, but no pores beyond approximately 10 nm. In agreement, derived pore size distributions reveal micro- and small mesopores but lack an indication of large mesopores of 20–50 nm (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>B), suggesting that these voids are isolated from each other and inaccessible to nitrogen, which is astonishing in light of the quite high template-to-precursor ratio.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/asset/images/medium/cm3c01520_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/asset/images/large/cm3c01520_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (A) Nitrogen isotherms of synthesized lignin-based carbon and (B) derived pore size distributions by applying a nonlocal density functional theory (NLDFT) kernel for carbon with cylindrical pore geometry to the adsorption branch.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/asset/images/large/cm3c01520_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To address this apparent discrepancy between physisorption and SEM/TEM results for carbons templated with PEO<sub><i>n</i></sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub><i>m</i></sub> polymers, SAXS was utilized as a tool to detect all mesopores, both accessible and isolated ones. In the following, carbon templated with PEO<sub>214</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>322</sub> was disregarded due to its high polydispersity of pore sizes indicated by TEM and SEM; thus, focusing on carbons templated with PEO<sub>251</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>95</sub> and PEO<sub>428</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>265</sub>. The SAXS patterns of both samples (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>A and <b>D</b>) exhibit a series of distinct, broad maxima/oscillations, pointing to ordered arrays of mesopores. The first maximum at <i>s</i> = 0.043 nm<sup>–1</sup> (PEO<sub>251</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>95</sub>) and <i>s</i> = 0.021 nm<sup>–1</sup> (PEO<sub>428</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>265</sub>) can be interpreted as a “Bragg” reflection, <i>i.e</i>., caused by a defined average pore-to-pore distance. At larger <i>s</i> for both carbons, broad maxima/oscillations were observed, which might be interpreted as “Bragg” maxima or oscillations of the form factor of defined objects. Interestingly, an almost perfect Porod law is observed at larger <i>s</i> (<i>I</i>(<i>s</i>) ∝ 1/<i>s</i><sup>4</sup>) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/suppl_file/cm3c01520_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11C and S11F</a>), confirming a well-defined two-phase system of larger mesopores/voids and the carbon matrix.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/asset/images/medium/cm3c01520_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/asset/images/large/cm3c01520_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (A, D) Experimental SAXS data <i>I</i>(<i>s</i>) and fits (solid line) based on the Percus–Yevick approach with <i>s</i> = 2 sin(θ) λ<sup>–1</sup>, as well as (B, E) obtained number distributions of pore diameters induced by each respective template. Furthermore, chord length distributions obtained by converting experimental SAXS data fit for (C) PEO<sub>251</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>95</sub>-templated and (F) PEO<sub>428</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>265</sub>-templated mesoporous carbon. Contributions evoked by penetration of micropores and small mesopores (approximately &lt;5 nm, yellow), pore walls (orange), and large templated mesopores (red), as well as pore-to-pore distances (cyan) are color-coded and sketched in a scheme. In the scheme, microporosity was omitted for the sake of clarity.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/asset/images/large/cm3c01520_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">A quantitative SAXS evaluation of pore sizes upon scattering methods depends on the assumption of a geometrical model in order to fit the experimental data. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> As TEM micrographs (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>) display spherical pores lacking a closed-packed arrangement with crystalline long-range order, a SAXS model on either a crystal-like array or on local order might be feasible. In the following, an approach was applied for the semiquantitative modeling of the SAXS data following the Percus–Yevick structure factor for hard spheres, as successfully applied previously. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> Aside from a hard-sphere potential, the Percus–Yevick structure factor presents a practical advantage by relying on only two parameters: the hard-sphere potential radius <i>R</i> and volume fraction η. Separated spherical pores thus fulfill a basic precondition of the Percus–Yevick approach, and our approach uses a polydisperse form factor for spheres. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32 ref36 ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref36 ref37" aria-label="reference 32, 36 and 37">(32,36,37)</a> Modeling of the experimental SAXS data using this Percus–Yevick decoupling approach is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>A,<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>D. The overall shape of the SAXS curves is modeled quite well, although the position of the higher maxima cannot be achieved precisely. Yet, a reasonable pore diameter distribution was obtained for each mesoporous carbon material (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>B,<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>E). The pore diameters of (22 ± 3) nm for PEO<sub>251</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>95</sub>-templated carbon and (37 ± 5) nm for PEO<sub>428</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>265</sub>-templated carbon are in good agreement with the TEM results. This SAXS analysis furthermore provides volume fractions of 44% for PEO<sub>251</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>95</sub>-templated carbon and correspondingly 35% for PEO<sub>428</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>265</sub>-templated carbon as well, which are in qualitative agreement with SEM/TEM analysis regarding the order of magnitude. Interestingly, the SAXS evaluation confirms a broader pore size distribution with an increasing block length of the applied polymer, in agreement with the TEM, SEM, and DLS analysis. In addition, we have modeled the SAXS patterns assuming a face-centered cubic (FCC) array of polydisperse spheres (see the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/suppl_file/cm3c01520_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI</a> file, Figure S12). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> It is seen that the position of the maxima can be adequately modeled as the higher-order reflections of the FCC packing, indicating a three-dimensional ordered packing, while it is not possible to fit the overall course of the curve. The average mesopore diameters obtained from this procedure and the Percus–Yevick-based approach agree reasonably well with each other for both materials (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/suppl_file/cm3c01520_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S3</a>). Yet, the wall thickness between these isolated mesopores cannot be calculated with acceptable accuracy, as the value of the average pore-to-pore diameter shows substantial uncertainty, being severely dependent on the packing model.</div><div class="NLM_p">Thus, an alternative SAXS evaluation was performed that is not based on a distinct type of packing, namely, by the concept of chord length distributions (CLD). Since Porod′s law is valid for the SAXS data of both materials, corresponding to a two-phase system, the evaluation of the CLD is meaningful. Without any assumption of a geometrical model, CLD analysis presents a powerful tool to examine nanostructured materials with only local mesoscopic order. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26 ref38 ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref38 ref39" aria-label="reference 26, 38 and 39">(26,38,39)</a> The CLDs evaluated by fitting the experimental SAXS data with analytical basic functions are displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>C (PEO<sub>251</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>95</sub>-templated) and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>F (PEO<sub>428</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>265</sub>-templated). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> The parametrization process is demonstrated in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/suppl_file/cm3c01520_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/suppl_file/cm3c01520_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI File Figure S11</a>). As a result of the absence of a sufficient amount of data points at very small scattering vectors <i>s</i>, causing a mathematical obstacle for the fitting procedure, artifacts appear in both CLDs at large radii (discussion provided in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/suppl_file/cm3c01520_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI</a>, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/suppl_file/cm3c01520_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13</a>). As a superposition of linear distances between phase boundaries, positive, respectively negative, contributions reflect the chord penetration of one, respectively two, interphases. Though this does not equal a pore size distribution, together with TEM as an independent method, it is justifiable to interpret the first positive contributions in both CLDs to be caused by varying pore diameters and wall thicknesses. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26 ref36 ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref36 ref39" aria-label="reference 26, 36 and 39">(26,36,39)</a> According to Smarsly et al., <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> assigning the contributions of micropores and small mesopores (approximately &lt;5 nm), pore walls, and large templated mesopores is even possible. In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>C,<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>F, the respective contributions are color-coded in yellow, orange, and red.</div><div class="NLM_p">As a main benefit, the CLD allows for the detection of small entities such as micropores and small mesopores. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36 ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36 ref39" aria-label="reference 36 and 39">(36,39)</a> Compared to previously published block copolymer-templated material, the CLDs of the PEO<sub>251</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>95</sub>- and PEO<sub>428</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>265</sub>-based carbons exhibit only small contributions of entities below approximately 5 nm, i.e., attributable to small pores or thin pore walls. Regardless of this assignment, one can safely state that the materials contain a quite small concentration of micropores or mesopores between 2 and 5 nm (accessible or inaccessible). This finding can further explain the low degree of connectivity between the larger spherical mesopores. Large mesopore contributions in an <i>x</i>-range of approximately 18 to 22 nm for PEO<sub>251</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>95</sub>-templated carbon and approximately 32 to 42 nm for PEO<sub>428</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>265</sub>-templated carbon are in accordance with averaged pore diameter measurements in TEM micrographs. Reasonable values corresponding to TEM measurements are also obtained by the first minimum in the CLDs. For PEO<sub>251</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>95</sub>-templated carbon, the sum of pore diameters and wall thickness determined by TEM corresponds to a pore-to-pore diameter of (28 ± 6) nm. Hence, the minimum in the CLD at 27 nm as the pore-to-pore distance is in good agreement. The CLD of carbon templated with PEO<sub>428</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>265</sub> with a minimum at 50 nm shows a greater deviation from TEM results of (44 ± 8) nm pore-to-pore diameter but is still within standard deviations.</div><div class="NLM_p">Concluding pore morphology analysis, TEM, SEM, nitrogen physisorption, and SAXS analysis provide a coherent analysis, proving well-defined nanoscaled voids for all templates. In particular, PEO<sub><i>n</i></sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub><i>m</i></sub> polymers proved themselves as applicable templates, generating spherical mesopores of defined diameter and packing. Keeping a constant mass ratio of carbon precursor to template, PEO<sub><i>n</i></sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub><i>m</i></sub> polymers introduced isolated spherical pores in contrast to accessible pore morphologies by well-established Pluronic F-127 or PIB<sub>50</sub>-<i>b</i>-PEO<sub>45</sub> templates as outlined with physisorption and SAXS experiments.</div><div class="NLM_p">Next to the examination of the pore morphology, a thorough analysis of the carbon microstructure, <i>i.e</i>., of the graphenes and their stacking, is crucial in several ways as well. The dimension and disorder of the graphenes and their stacks (in the following termed “carbon microstructure”) might be as relevant for carbons applied in various energy material applications as the pore structure, especially for the transport of sodium in hard carbons. Here, the average interlayer spacing <i>a</i><sub>3</sub> presents a valuable parameter that is related to the (de)sodiation capability of sodium and is important for the elucidation of the mechanism of sodium storage in hard carbons, as elaborated in the recent literature. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23 ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref41" aria-label="reference 23 and 41">(23,41)</a> Moreover, a precise characterization of the carbon microstructure is a prerequisite to separate its impact from the impact of the pore morphology (diameter, shape, accessibility, and mutual connection) on electrochemical parameters. Also, investigating the general impact of introducing a template in combination with a carbonization process might reveal intriguing insights about the formation and reorganization of nanosized graphene sheets within nongraphitic carbons during thermal treatment. Therefore, microstructure elucidation by Raman spectroscopy and WAXS will be discussed, focusing on eight carbon samples produced with four different templates (nontemplated carbon, Pluronic F-127-templated carbon, PIB<sub>50</sub>-<i>b</i>-PEO<sub>45</sub>-templated carbon, and PEO<sub>428</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>265</sub>-templated carbon) applying two different carbonization temperatures (900 and 1300 °C). The temperature of 1300 °C was selected as the upper choice to ensure that the conductivity behavior of the samples met the requirements as anode materials in a sodium-ion battery, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> which will be discussed in detail later in this study. Preservation of the mesopore structure upon the additional carbonization at 1300 °C as evaluated for the samples carbonized at 900 °C was confirmed by TEM (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/suppl_file/cm3c01520_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S14</a>), proving the thermal stability of the mesoporous scaffold.</div><div class="NLM_p">In Raman spectroscopy analysis, by applying an excitation wavelength of 532 nm, characteristic first-order bands in the spectral range of 1000 cm<sup>–1</sup> to 1750 cm<sup>–1</sup> and second-order bands from 2250 to 3500 cm<sup>–1</sup> appear for all samples (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17 ref42 ref43 ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref42 ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 17 and 42 to 44">(17,42−44)</a> All bands are broad and overlap as it is known for low carbonization temperatures and disordered nongraphitic structures. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17 ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref43" aria-label="reference 17 and 43">(17,43)</a> For spectral parameter elucidation, fitting the first-order bands includes all five reported Raman bands (commonly termed D1, D2, D3, D4, and G) applied with Lorentzian line shapes. Intensity maxima arise from the defect-activated band D1 appearing at ∼1350 cm<sup>–1</sup> due to the A<sub>1g</sub> symmetric vibrational mode of graphene edge defects and G band at ∼1585 cm<sup>–1</sup> corresponding to a graphitic lattice mode with E<sub>2g</sub> symmetry. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17 ref42 ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref42 ref43" aria-label="reference 17, 42 and 43">(17,42,43)</a> Overlapping with the D3 band at ∼1500 cm<sup>–1</sup> and D4 band at ∼1200 cm<sup>–1</sup> appears in all recorded Raman spectra. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43 ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 43 and 44">(43,44)</a></div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/asset/images/medium/cm3c01520_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/asset/images/large/cm3c01520_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Raman spectra of (from top to bottom) nontemplated carbon, Pluronic F-127-templated carbon, PIB<sub>50</sub>-<i>b</i>-PEO<sub>45</sub>-templated carbon, PEO<sub>428</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>265</sub>-templated carbon carbonized at (B) 900 °C and (E) 1300 °C. Fittings of first-order bands at 900 °C carbonized (A) nontemplated and (C) PEO<sub>428</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>265</sub>-templated carbons. Likewise, fittings of nontemplated carbon first-order bands (D) and second-order bands (G), and PEO<sub>428</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>265</sub>-templated carbon first-order bands (F) and second-order bands (H) after heat treatment at 1300 °C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/asset/images/large/cm3c01520_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">For carbons carbonized at 900 °C, first-order band intensities and positions are qualitatively and quantitatively similar to the overview spectra (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>B) and in-detail fittings in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>A,<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>C. In contrast, differences among the samples appear at a carbonization temperature of 1300 °C. Whereas all templated carbon materials transform equally, as represented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>F, the first-order Raman spectra of the nontemplated carbon can be distinguished from all other samples upon such higher temperature treatment. With a pronounced asymmetric shape, the G mode overlaps with the D2 band appearing at ∼1620 cm<sup>–1</sup>, corresponding to the same graphitic lattice mode as the G band involving graphene layers at the surface (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>D). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> As known in the literature, the D2 band appears upon higher carbonization temperatures, inducing a more ordered graphene lattice. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16 ref43 ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref43 ref45" aria-label="reference 16, 43 and 45">(16,43,45)</a> Hence, notably, the appearance of the D2 band in nontemplated carbon carbonized at 1300 °C suggests qualitatively higher structural order compared to templated carbon. In addition, the increase in structural order is supported by the most pronounced decline of the full width at half-maximum (fwhm) within Raman modes of nontemplated lignin-derived carbon at 1300 °C, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43 ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43 ref17" aria-label="reference 43 and 17">(43,17)</a> which is further underlined by consideration of the second-order bands. As Raman spectra of all samples carbonized at 900 °C resemble one another in the spectral range from 2250 to 3500 cm<sup>–1</sup> (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/suppl_file/cm3c01520_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S15 and S16</a>), combinations of graphitic lattice vibration modes and overtones appear different for nontemplated carbon treated at 1300 °C (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>G) compared to all other templated samples (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>H). Fitting the second-order modes was applied by considering 2*D1, 2*D2, and 2*D4 overtones next to the G+D1 combination band with Lorentzian profiles each. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17 ref43 ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 17, 43 and 44">(17,43,44)</a> As the 2*D1 overtone, though splitting into two 2*D1<sub>1</sub> and 2*D1<sub>2</sub> peaks, appears in Raman spectra of graphite as well, the absence of or a low-intensity broad 2*D1 overtone points out a low degree of stacking within investigated templated carbon materials. In contrast, nontemplated carbon carbonized at 1300 °C exhibits a pronounced 2*D1 band shaped as a single Lorentzian, well-known for turbostratic nongraphitic carbon. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17 ref44 ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref44 ref46" aria-label="reference 17, 44 and 46">(17,44,46)</a> Next to comparing templated and nontemplated lignin-derived carbon, an appealing comparison can be made with nontemplated lignin-phloroglucinol-based carbon (this study) and phenol-formaldehyde resin-based carbons (PF-R) reported by Schuepfer et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> For PF-R materials, the appearance of the 2*D1 band was first observed upon carbonization at 1800 °C. The presence of the aforementioned band at a significantly lower carbonization temperature of 1300 °C within investigated lignin-based carbons suggests an accelerated growth and increase in structural order of the graphenes compared to conventional resin-based carbons. In a qualitative comparison of Raman spectra alone, decreasing fwhm and increasing D2 and 2*D1 band intensity of nontemplated lignin-derived carbons are clear indicators of decreasing disorder and layer extension increase compared to all templated carbons. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16 ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 16 and 17">(16,17)</a> Hence, it can be stated already that introducing templates causes restriction in graphene layer (re)organization during thermal treatment. Also, all different polymers seem to affect and hinder graphitization to the same extent.</div><div class="NLM_p">For quantitative carbon microstructure analysis by Raman spectroscopy, several empirical relations determining the graphene extension <i>L</i><sub>a</sub> based on the intensity ratio of D1 and G band are described and applied in the literature. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47 ref48 ref49 ref50'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47 ref48 ref49 ref50" aria-label="reference 47 to 50">(47−50)</a> Though, as shown in extensive comparative studies by Schuepfer et al., <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> the commonly used Raman intensity ratio <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> does not provide a universal correlation to determine <i>L</i><sub>a</sub>. For instance, since the D band intensity depends on formed defects, whose type and density are related to precursor molecules and carbonization procedure, quantitative evaluation of Raman results ideally requires a combination with other methods determining the graphene extension <i>L</i><sub>a</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16 ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 16 and 17">(16,17)</a> Several quantitative structural parameters can be obtained by wide-angle X-ray scattering (WAXS) analysis, which was applied to the carbon materials studied here. It is important to note that the overall class of nongraphitic carbons, to which hard carbons belong, shows turbostratic disorder in the graphene lattice and lacks crystallographic three-dimensional long-range order within the stacking of graphene layers, according to the IUPAC definition. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref51'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51" aria-label="reference 51">(51)</a> An appropriate carbon microstructure evaluation based on the diffuse and overlapping (00<i>l</i>) and (<i>hk</i>) reflections of asymmetric shape in the obtained WAXS pattern (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>) was possible by applying the algorithm developed by Ruland and Smarsly <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> implemented in the <i>OctCarb</i> script developed by Osswald and Smarsly <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> in combination with the GNU <i>Octave</i> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a> software. The approach by Ruland and Smarsly <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> is based on fitting the experimental WAXS data over the whole angular range by a continuous model function obtaining up to 18 physical and geometrical parameters describing the carbon microstructure, especially in terms of dimension and disorder of the graphenes and their stacking. The theory of the WAXS algorithm is described in detail elsewhere. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21" aria-label="reference 18 to 21">(18−21)</a> The obtained experimental WAXS data with corresponding fittings of the studied lignin-derived carbons carbonized at 900 and 1300 °C are demonstrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/asset/images/medium/cm3c01520_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/asset/images/large/cm3c01520_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. WAXS data of (from top to bottom) nontemplated carbon, Pluronic F-127-templated carbon, PIB<sub>50</sub>-<i>b</i>-PEO<sub>45</sub>-templated carbon, PEO<sub>428</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>265</sub>-templated carbon, and fitting curves (solid line) obtained by the algorithm of Ruland and Smarsly <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> as a function of the modulus of the scattering vector <i>s</i> = 2 sin(θ) λ<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/asset/images/large/cm3c01520_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Already, the qualitative comparison of the WAXS patterns alone is in accordance with insights obtained by Raman spectroscopy: Broad reflections govern the experimental WAXS data for all investigated carbons, but the nontemplated carbon carbonized at 1300 °C (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>, top right) presents a quite interesting exception. Here, significantly sharper reflections are obtained. Whereas the (004) reflection can only be perceived as asymmetry or shoulder in the vicinity of the (10) reflection in all other patterns due to a large stacking disorder, it appears defined and isolated in the WAXS data of 1300 °C carbonized and nontemplated lignin-derived carbon, indicating a higher order within the graphene layers. Going beyond such qualitative analysis, the fitting approach by Osswald and Smarsly <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> was successfully applied to the WAXS data, achieving reasonable fitting over almost the entire experimental WAXS range. Hence, the structural parameters derived from them can be regarded as reliable. The most important obtained parameters regarding the size of graphene layers and stacking and respective disorder parameters are listed in <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl2" href="#tbl2">Table 2</a> and schematically illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>. Interestingly, the qualitative interpretation of Raman spectroscopy and WAXS data is supported by the determination of quantitative structure parameters based on the WAXS fitting approach. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a></div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/asset/images/medium/cm3c01520_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/asset/images/large/cm3c01520_0007.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. Schematic Illustration of most relevant layer and stacking parameters describing turbostratic nongraphitic carbon.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/asset/images/large/cm3c01520_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl2"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 2. class="NLM_table-wrap-foot" id="IDtable-wrap-foot-t2fn1"><div class="footnote" id="t2fn1"><sup><sup>a</sup></sup><p class="last">Except for the nontemplated carbon carbonized at 1300 °C, due to the absence of higher-order reflections, values of stacking parameters are to be considered with caution, as explained below.</p></div></div></div><div class="NLM_p">Multiple comparisons can be drawn. First, an analysis of the layer-related parameters reveals that increasing the carbonization temperature leads to a higher degree of intralayer order within the carbon microstructure. This is evidenced by the enlarged average graphene layer extent (<i>L</i><sub>a</sub>) and reduced disorder parameter (σ<sub>1</sub>) observed across all carbon materials if the two treatment temperatures are compared, i.e., 900 °C vs 1300 °C. A significantly lower increase in <i>L</i><sub>a</sub> (8–17%) was observed across all templated mesoporous carbon materials compared to a much larger growth in <i>L</i><sub>a</sub> (from 2.8 nm to approximately 6 nm, i.e., by 125%) for the nontemplated carbon. Similarly, σ<sub>1</sub> decreased by 46% in nontemplated compared to 8–29% in templated carbon. Additionally, an extended average C–C bond length <i>l</i><sub>cc</sub> approaching the value of 1.421 Å in graphite <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref53'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53" aria-label="reference 53">(53)</a> is observed for nontemplated carbon, while the <i>l</i><sub>cc</sub> values of templated carbons are kept rather constant. Therefore, while the microstructures of all carbons carbonized at 900 °C are similar, further graphene growth and increasing order upon higher carbonization temperatures are significantly impeded when polymers are used as soft templates. Overall, this detailed analysis reveals the interesting insight that the templating of carbon has an impact on the microstructure, even when high-temperature post-treatments are carried out after template removal.</div><div class="NLM_p">As mentioned before, not all WAXS reflections are resolved well due to the intrinsic properties of nongraphitic carbons and a substantial disorder. Yet, valid structure parameters depending on the signal width are only obtained by the applied evaluation technique if at least two reflections of the same lattice plane family caused by intralayer, respectively interlayer, scattering can be fitted precisely, i.e., for instance, the (10) and (20) reflections. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> Considering the (00<i>l</i>) reflections, this is not the case, as noted for the (004) reflection. Together with the finite measurement range of standard WAXS laboratory setups limiting the number of recorded reflections, this shows the limitations of possible microstructure evaluation within this study, causing the stacking height <i>L</i><sub>c</sub> only to be determined in terms of order of magnitude. Again, the nontemplated carbon sample carbonized at 1300 °C is an exception here due to more advanced structural ordering in the two-dimensional graphene layers and their stacking. Regarding the average interlayer spacing <i>a</i><sub>3</sub>, the value is usually determined directly from the position of the (002) signal maximum by Bragg′s law. Such procedure, however, involves significant uncertainties and yields unrealistic values, <i>e.g</i>., ∼ 3.8 Å for templated carbons carbonized at 1300 °C (<a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl3" href="#tbl3">Table 3</a>). By contrast, the WAXS fitting procedure takes into account effects possibly influencing the signal position and width, in particular the distribution of <i>a</i><sub>3</sub>, but also small-angle scattering contributions, etc. The systematic overestimation of the interlayer spacing <i>a</i><sub>3</sub> of nongraphitic carbons applying Bragg′s law already justifies the comprehensive WAXS analysis applying the fitting procedure and algorithm by Ruland and Smarsly, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> especially as mechanistic studies of hard carbon anodes for sodium storage still rely on the interlayer spacing <i>a</i><sub>3</sub> determined by Bragg′s law. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23 ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref41" aria-label="reference 23 and 41">(23,41)</a> The better the fitting of the whole WAXS curve, the more reliable the values for <i>a</i><sub>3</sub> are received. The correlation between the interlayer spacing <i>a</i><sub>3</sub> of hard carbons in SIBs and the electrochemical performance will be discussed later.</div><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl3"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 3. Overview of Structural Parameters of Nontemplated Carbon, Pluronic F-127-Templated Carbon, PIB<sub>50</sub>-b-PEO<sub>45</sub>-Templated Carbon, and PEO<sub>428</sub>-b-PHA<sub>265</sub>-Templated Carbon Carbonized at 1300 °C Applied as Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</div></div></div><div class="scrollable-table-wrap" style="display: block;"><table class="table "><colgroup><col aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a></th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char=".">av. interlayer spacing <i>a</i><sub>3</sub>/Å by Bragg′s law</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center">mean carbon mesopore diameter (TEM)/nm<a class="ref internalNav" href="#t3fn1" aria-label="a"><sup>a</sup></a></th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" area/m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">no template</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">3.45</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">3.45</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">62.5</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char"
