valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">pluronic&nbsp;F-127</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">3.52</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">3.75</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">27.8</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">∼10</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">5&nbsp;±&nbsp;1</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">1.27</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">496</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">PIB<sub>50</sub>-<i>b</i>-PEO<sub>45</sub></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">3.54</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">3.88</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">28.6</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">∼10</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">9&nbsp;±&nbsp;1</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">1.18</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">571</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">PEO<sub>428</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>265</sub></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">3.50</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">3.75</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">27.8</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">∼10</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">37&nbsp;±&nbsp;6</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">1.28</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">145</td></tr></tbody></table></div><div class="NLM_table-wrap-foot" id="IDtable-wrap-foot-t3fn1"><div class="footnote" id="t3fn1"><sup><sup>a</sup></sup><p class="last">Obtained by averaging TEM measurements.</p></div></div></div><div class="NLM_p">As an important advantage of this WAXS data evaluation, fitting of (10) and (11) intralayer reflections enabled a precise quantification of <i>L</i><sub>a</sub> and the parameter (σ<sub>1</sub>), which are meaningful parameters to describe the graphene layers. Thereby, the recently introduced advanced Raman spectroscopy analysis correlating spectroscopic quantities against WAXS analysis, introduced by Schuepfer et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> and elaborated by Osswald et al., <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> is applicable. Therein, the dependency of the G band position on the average layer extension <i>L</i><sub>a</sub> was discussed with empirical and theoretical relationships. As the G band position is nondispersive, comparing the Raman and WAXS analysis results in this study with the universal master curves of the above-mentioned literature <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16 ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 16 and 17">(16,17)</a> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>) allows further assessment of the validity of microstructure analysis of lignin-based carbons.</div><figure id="fig8" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 8</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/asset/images/medium/cm3c01520_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/asset/images/large/cm3c01520_0008.jpeg" id="gr8" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 8. Relation of G band position to obtained graphene extension <i>L</i><sub>a</sub> acquired by WAXS (Schuepfer et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> and this study) and WANS (Osswald et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a>) of lignin-derived carbons (this study) and comparable PF-R carbons (Schuepfer et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> and Osswald et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a>). Reprinted or adapted with permission under a Creative Commons CC-BY 4.0 from ref  <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> and ref  <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a>. Copyright 2023 MDPI and 2020 Elsevier.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/asset/images/large/cm3c01520_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520&amp;id=fig8"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The results of the current study are in good agreement with those of previous ones on comparable PF-R carbons, which belong to the class of hard carbons, too. It is worth mentioning that wide-angle neutron scattering (WANS) and the results applied by Osswald et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> provide a higher accuracy compared to WAXS analysis (Schuepfer et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> and this study) due to significant damping of WAXS data at larger <i>s</i> values caused by the atomic form factors, the impact of Compton scattering, and confined scattering range of <i>s</i> in typical Cu-Kα-WAXS laboratory instruments. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> Typically, <i>L</i><sub>a</sub> is overestimated, and σ<sub>1</sub> shifted to higher values for such typical XRD setups. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16 ref54'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref54" aria-label="reference 16 and 54">(16,54)</a> Pfaff et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref54'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54" aria-label="reference 54">(54)</a> specified a maximum deviation of approximately 25% as the error range, determining <i>L</i><sub>a</sub> with standard laboratory WAXS setups. Considering this, corresponding error bars complement the obtained <i>L</i><sub>a</sub> values in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>, representing microstructure analysis of the mesoporous lignin-derived carbons. Approaching the WANS-based master curve of Osswald et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> shows a good validness of the conducted microstructure analysis.</div><div class="NLM_p">With the knowledge gained about the carbon microstructure, density measurements by helium pycnometry (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/suppl_file/cm3c01520_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S17</a>) can also be evaluated validly. First, a density value of 1.76 g cm<sup>–3</sup> for the nontemplated carbon material treated at 900 °C is in accordance with other comparable hard carbons treated at similar carbonization temperatures known in the literature as in Badaczewski et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> or Kubota et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref55'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55" aria-label="reference 55">(55)</a> For 900 °C as carbonization temperature, microstructure analysis revealed a comparable level of graphitization and a similar interlayer distance <i>a</i><sub>3</sub> for all samples, including the nontemplated carbon material. Hence, following the microstructure, templated carbons are expected to exhibit the same density value as the nontemplated carbon. However, a successive decrease in density is observed as templates are introduced with the lowest values for PEO<sub><i>n</i></sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub><i>m</i></sub>-templated carbons. This can be explained by the extensive introduction of closed pores that are inaccessible to helium. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18 ref56'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref56" aria-label="reference 18 and 56">(18,56)</a> The measured density is therefore not the density of the carbon skeleton only, but includes inaccessible pores. This further supports the existence of closed porosity most pronounced within the PEO<sub><i>n</i></sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub><i>m</i></sub>-templated carbon materials, as pointed out with physisorption and SAXS analysis, already. For further discussion of density values, reference is made to the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/suppl_file/cm3c01520_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI</a>.</div><div class="NLM_p">Concluding the microstructure analysis, the most pronounced level of graphene order is observed for the nontemplated carbon materials carbonized at 1300 °C and strongly underlined in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 9</a> (TEM) at first sight. Moreover, the complementary Raman and WAXS analysis demonstrates that all investigated mesoporous carbons resemble one another, especially upon applying the same carbonization temperature in their graphene structure, independent of the kind of polymer that was used as the soft template. These findings can be confirmed visually by high-resolution TEM images of nontemplated carbon, Pluronic F-127-templated carbon, PIB<sub>50</sub>-<i>b</i>-PEO<sub>45</sub>-templated carbon, and PEO<sub>428</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>265</sub>-templated carbon carbonized at 1300 °C (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 9</a>). In contrast to templated carbon materials, the nontemplated carbon sample exhibits aligned graphene layers after carbonization at 1300 °C, demonstrating a higher level of graphene order governing the carbon microstructure. Having addressed the influence of templating on the carbon transformation and, more importantly, confirmed the comparability of all mesoporous lignin-derived carbons in terms of their carbon microstructures, investigations of the pore morphology-induced effects in several energy material applications can take place.</div><figure id="fig9" data-index="9" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 9</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/asset/images/medium/cm3c01520_0009.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/asset/images/large/cm3c01520_0009.jpeg" id="gr9" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 9. High-resolution TEM images of (A) nontemplated carbon, (B) Pluronic F-127-templated carbon, (C) PIB<sub>50</sub>-<i>b</i>-PEO<sub>45</sub>-templated carbon, and (D) PEO<sub>428</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>265</sub>-templated carbon carbonized at 1300 °C. The pronounced level of graphitization and more ordered graphene layer organization is visible in the nontemplated carbon material and highlighted in red.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/asset/images/large/cm3c01520_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520&amp;id=fig9"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">As previously mentioned, nontemplated carbon revealed the highest degree of order of the graphene structure and the most compact microstructure, applying a carbonization temperature of 1300 °C. Meanwhile, the microstructure of all templated samples remains consistent at the same carbonization temperature, regardless of the templates used. The main distinguishing factor between these templated carbon materials is their mesopore size and structure, with some possessing interconnected pores/channels (PIB<sub>50</sub>-<i>b</i>-PEO<sub>45</sub>-templated and Pluronic F-127-templated carbon) and others exhibiting isolated closed pores (PEO<sub>428</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>265</sub>-templated carbon). Finally, for characterization, we investigated the performance of four different carbon materials (nontemplated carbon, Pluronic F-127-templated carbon, PIB<sub>50</sub>-<i>b</i>-PEO<sub>45</sub>-templated carbon, and PEO<sub>428</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>265</sub>-templated carbon, all carbonized at 1300 °C) as anode materials for sodium-ion batteries to help understand the sodium storage requirement regarding porosity and bulk phase. Therefore, an overview of the determined structure parameters for the respective carbons is given in <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl3" href="#tbl3">Table 3</a>. As discussed before, the intensity ratio of the D1 and G bands is still applied for quantitative structure analysis in the literature. Hence, this value is displayed in <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl3" href="#tbl3">Table 3</a>, as well. Although the nontemplated carbon exhibits a much larger level of “graphitization”, <i>I</i><sub>D1</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> values of templated carbons are similar. Again, this underlines that the determination of graphene layer extent <i>L</i><sub>a</sub> from the <i>I</i><sub>D1</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> value has to be interpreted with care, as demonstrated recently by Schuepfer et al., <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> which justifies our intensive state-of-the-art evaluation based on WAXS as complementary analysis.</div><div class="NLM_p">The initial three charge–discharge profiles of all half cells at a current density of 30 mA g<sup>–1</sup> are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 10</a>A–<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">10</a>D. As anticipated, the half cell with highly graphitized nontemplated carbon as anode material revealed negligible capacity due to the oriented graphite domain (schematically illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 10</a>I) with the smallest average interlayer distances (<a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl3" href="#tbl3">Table 3</a>), confirming the inability of sodium to form graphite intercalation compounds. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref57'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57" aria-label="reference 57">(57)</a> Recent research into the mechanism of sodium-ion batteries has shown that different observed capacities can be attributed to sodium behavior. Specifically, capacities above approximately 0.1 V (sloping potential region) result from a combination of energetically different processes as well as the continuous structural distortion arising from the interlayer expansion that accompanies the insertion of sodium ions. As schematically illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 10</a>I, small interlayer distances (<a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl3" href="#tbl3">Table 3</a>) prevent sodium intercalation into the graphitic layers, causing very low capacity in the high-potential region already. Only sodium adsorption on the surface might be possible. For templated carbon materials, the interlayer spacing <i>a</i><sub>3</sub> is larger (<a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl3" href="#tbl3">Table 3</a>), and as a result, a pronounced sloping capacity is observable (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 10</a>B–<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">10</a>D). As the values of the interlayer spacing <i>a</i><sub>3</sub> and the yielded sloping capacity are comparable for all materials regardless of the applied template, the assignment of intercalation being responsible for the sloping capacity is reasonable and in accordance with the results of Anji Reddy et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref58'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref58" aria-label="reference 58">(58)</a> Enabled sodium intercalation for all templated carbon materials is illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 10</a>J–<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">10</a>L. As mentioned previously, in comparison to the average interlayer spacing <i>a</i><sub>3</sub> determined by Bragg′s law, a consequent overestimation of <i>a</i><sub>3</sub> can be observed for the templated carbon materials. Only for nontemplated carbon do the values of interlayer spacing <i>a</i><sub>3</sub> determined by applying the algorithm of Ruland and Smarsly <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> and Bragg′s law equal each other as the inaccuracy in determining <i>a</i><sub>3</sub> by Bragg′s law declines the more graphitic the carbon structure becomes. For templated nongraphitic carbon, comparison with the more accurately determined values by the algorithm of Ruland and Smarsly <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> and resulting sloping capacities indicate that also smaller interlayer distances than the often stated 3.6 Å threshold distance <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref58'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref58" aria-label="reference 58">(58)</a> allow sodium to intercalate. So far, experimental studies determining the threshold interlayer distance for sodium intercalation rely on the position of the (002) reflection and Bragg′s law. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23 ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref41" aria-label="reference 23 and 41">(23,41)</a> Here, our study raises attention to the fact that interlayer distances for nongraphitic carbons exhibiting broad reflections in WAXS experiments determined by Bragg′s law need to be handled with caution and eventually reevaluated.</div><figure id="fig10" data-index="10" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 10</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/asset/images/medium/cm3c01520_0010.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/asset/images/large/cm3c01520_0010.jpeg" id="gr10" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 10. Discharge/charge profiles of sodium-ion batteries with (A) nontemplated carbon, (B) Pluronic F-127-templated carbon, (C) PIB<sub>50</sub>-<i>b</i>-PEO<sub>45</sub>-templated carbon, and (D) PEO<sub>428</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>265</sub>-templated carbon as anode materials. The highest specific capacity with plateau capacity is observed only with PEO<sub>428</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>265</sub>-templated carbon as anode materials with closed pores. Additionally, (E–H) cyclic voltammograms for the batteries with each respective anode material. Below, applied carbons in each sodium-ion battery are drawn schematically (I–L), illustrating carbon microstructure, which is comparable for all templated carbons, and the different pore morphologies. Interparticle voids, pore sizes, and pore connections do not have the claim to be represented true to scale.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/asset/images/large/cm3c01520_0010.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520&amp;id=fig10"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">In contrast to the sloping capacities, the plateau capacities in the low potential region differ from each other. For the closed pore PEO<sub>428</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>265</sub>-templated carbon (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 10</a>L), the half cell exhibited the highest specific capacity among all, with the plateau capacity observed exclusively here. As the comprehensive microstructure analysis revealed, only the pore morphology distinguishes the templated carbon materials, which permits justifying the difference in the plateau capacity with the porosity of the carbons. Agreeing with our observation here, the plateau capacities below 0.1 V are attributed to sodium filling into closed pores (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 10</a>L) in the literature. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref58 ref59'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref58 ref59" aria-label="reference 58 and 59">(58,59)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The pore filling by sodium is further supported by the peak observed at approximately 0.25 V in cyclic voltammetry, which is absent for Pluronic F-127-templated and PIB<sub>50</sub>-<i>b</i>-PEO<sub>45</sub>-templated carbon as the anode material (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 10</a>E–<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">10</a>H). Also, the rate performances of all samples from 30 to 600 mA g<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig11" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 11</a>) are in accordance with the pore filling of Na<sup>+</sup> exclusively within PEO<sub>428</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>265</sub>-templated carbon with closed pores as anode material. The half cell with nontemplated carbon shows relatively low capacity mainly due to its more developed graphitic structure and narrow interlayer distance, as discussed previously. Among those templated carbons, PEO<sub>428</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>265</sub>-templated carbon as the anode material shows the highest capacity in all different currents. The higher capacity is mainly attributed to the pore filling of Na<sup>+</sup> from the plateau, which is not observed in other samples. Even after 900 cycles, the half cell with PEO<sub>428</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>265</sub>-templated carbon exhibits the highest specific capacity in long-term cycling performance tests (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/suppl_file/cm3c01520_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S18</a>).</div><figure id="fig11" data-index="11" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 11</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/asset/images/medium/cm3c01520_0011.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/asset/images/large/cm3c01520_0011.jpeg" id="gr11" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 11. Rate performance of sodium-ion batteries with nontemplated carbon, Pluronic F-127-templated carbon, PIB<sub>50</sub>-<i>b</i>-PEO<sub>45</sub>-templated carbon, and PEO<sub>428</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>265</sub>-templated carbon as anode materials at different current densities of 30, 60, 150, 300, and 600 mA g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/asset/images/large/cm3c01520_0011.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520&amp;id=fig11"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig12" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 12</a> shows the calculated diffusion coefficients of Na<sup>+</sup> (<i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub>) in the hard carbon anode from the GITT tests for sodiation. For completeness, calculated <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> from GITT tests for desodiation can be found in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/suppl_file/cm3c01520_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI</a>, Figure S19. It is worth noting that due to the intrinsic electrochemically active surface being different and difficult to measure, in this work, the electrode area was used in the GITT equation (see <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sec2" aria-label="experimental methods">experimental methods</a>). Thus, the absolute <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> may not be able to be compared between different samples, but it indicates a trend of <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> as a function of the potential. Together with the discharge/charge curve, the pore-filling mechanism was reported to be associated with an increase in <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> at the end of sodiation. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25 ref60 ref61'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref60 ref61" aria-label="reference 25, 60 and 61">(25,60,61)</a> From <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig12" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 12</a>, only with the PEO<sub>428</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>265</sub>-templated carbon as the anode material is such a trend visible; hence, it is again reasonable to further conclude the Na<sup>+</sup> pore-filling mechanism.</div><figure id="fig12" data-index="12" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 12</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/asset/images/medium/cm3c01520_0012.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/asset/images/large/cm3c01520_0012.jpeg" id="gr12" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 12. Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion coefficients obtained from GITT tests for sodiation during the second cycle for sodium-ion batteries with nontemplated carbon, Pluronic F-127-templated carbon, PIB<sub>50</sub>-<i>b</i>-PEO<sub>45</sub>-templated carbon, and PEO<sub>428</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>265</sub>-templated carbon as anode materials.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/asset/images/large/cm3c01520_0012.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520&amp;id=fig12"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Additionally, half cells prepared with PEO<sub>428</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>265</sub>-templated carbon material exhibited the highest initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of 67.7% while using Pluronic F-127-templated and PIB<sub>50</sub>-<i>b</i>-PEO<sub>45</sub>-templated carbon showed ICE values of 17.6 and 21.7%, respectively. This difference can be attributed to the surface area of the samples, where the higher surface area (<a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl3" href="#tbl3">Table 3</a>) leads to the increased and excessive formation of undesired solid-electrolyte interphase (SEI) layers, resulting in decreased ICE. Therefore, the aim for rational anode material design is a high porosity consisting of pores being inaccessible for the electrolyte exclusively. In this way, porosity does not lead to an increased accessible surface area but contributes positively to enhanced electrochemical performances, as elucidated. In the future, accessibility of the pores should be further minimized while maintaining a high closed porosity, which could be attempted by adjusting the amount of polymer template, for instance. While introducing closed mesoporosity, another important parameter needs to be considered. Referring back to helium pycnometry density measurements (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/suppl_file/cm3c01520_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S17</a>), a lower density is a consequence as well, which can downgrade the volumetric capacity. Anyway, studies in the literature <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref62'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref62" aria-label="reference 62">(62)</a> correlate a low density caused by introduced closed porosity with high plateau capacities of the respective half cell. Also, tested half cells with nontemplated carbon as anode materials showed only very poor performance, although exhibiting the highest density among the studied carbon materials. Hence, a correlation between a high density and good battery performance is not straightforward. Rather, a trade-off between a high density as a prerequisite for high volumetric capacities and maximized number of closed pores needs to be found for optimized anode materials.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Conclusively, with our comparative study applying different pore structures in hard carbon anode materials, the proposed mechanistic concept of sodium-ion storage by Au et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> could be supported, and the importance of in-detail carbon morphology and microstructure analysis toward the understanding and development of more efficient energy materials is underlined.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i18">Conclusion and Outlook</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, we demonstrated poly(ethylene oxide)-<i>b</i>-poly(hexyl acrylate) (PEO<sub><i>n</i></sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub><i>m</i></sub>) block copolymers as suitable templates for the synthesis of lignin-derived mesoporous carbons featuring quite ordered arrays of spherical mesopores. Pore diameters of approximately 20–50 nm were feasible with the precise variation of the polymer block lengths <i>m</i>, mainly. Applying identical ratios between carbon precursors and templates PEO<sub><i>n</i></sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub><i>m</i></sub> block copolymers induced isolated spherical pores unlike well-established Pluronic F-127 or PIB<sub>50</sub>-<i>b</i>-PEO<sub>45</sub> templates creating a smaller accessible channel or spherical pores, as proved by SAXS, SEM/TEM, and physisorption analysis. In future studies, systematically reducing the amount of templates might optimize the isolated character of pores regardless of the applied template and enable more efficient use of the polymer templates. Furthermore, in-depth microstructure evaluation with Raman spectroscopy and an advanced WAXS analysis of lignin-derived carbons was carried out to elucidate the transformation of the lignin-based precursor into graphene stacks. Notably, we find that the average interlayer spacing <i>a</i><sub>3</sub>, obtained by the advanced WAXS data fitting approach, is smaller than the values determined by simple single-peak analysis (Scherrer analysis), which can serve as an important finding with respect to theoretical models for (de)sodiation in hard carbons. Regardless of the type of block copolymer applied as a template, surprisingly, templated mesoporous carbons exhibited a comparable microstructure in terms of evaluated graphene layer and stacking parameters after carbonization at the same temperature. Comparison to nontemplated carbon revealed that the usage of different soft templates hampers the graphitization process upon heat treatment significantly. The in-depth analysis of the carbon microstructure thus allowed us to separate the influence of pore morphology in synthesized nongraphitic carbons applied in sodium-ion batteries. Due to the feasible tunability of the pore size and their accessibility within our carbon materials, we created a series of excellent model materials for various electrochemical applications. Our preliminary results on half-cells, testing for applicability in sodium-ion batteries, reveal that PEO<sub>428</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>265</sub>-templated carbon with closed mesopores as anode material showed an exclusive plateau capacity next to the sloping capacity in charge/discharge profiles. Open pore structures within the anode material exhibited a sloping capacity solely, causing significantly lower initial Coulombic efficiencies. In future studies, varying pore diameters in closed pore structures will enlighten the ideal pore size for an enlarged plateau capacity, causing enhanced sodium-ion battery performances. As the importance of carbon analysis for more effective energy materials was pointed out, further fundamental investigations might be initiated to enhance a detailed understanding of carbonization reactions of lignin-based carbons as well as the interaction and impact of different templates. Concluding, our synthesis of carbons with large mesopore sizes based on biomass-derived precursors opens great possibilities to enhance sustainability in various energy materials applications like electrocatalysis or energy storage.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-2"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i19">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Overview of applied amounts of reactants in polymer synthesis; <sup>1</sup>H NMR spectra of all macroinitiators and PEO<sub><i>n</i></sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub><i>m</i></sub> block copolymers; GPC RI traces of two macroinitiators and all PEO<sub><i>n</i></sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub><i>m</i></sub> block copolymers; DLS data of Pluronic F-127, PIB<sub>50</sub>-<i>b</i>-PEO<sub>45</sub>, and PEO<sub><i>n</i></sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub><i>m</i></sub> templates in methanol; detailed GITT curve for calculation; comparison of templated pore sizes determined by TEM and SEM; complete Raman spectra of nontemplated, Pluronic F-127-templated, PIB<sub>50</sub>-<i>b</i>-PEO<sub>45</sub>-templated, and PEO<sub>428</sub>-<i>b</i>-PHA<sub>265</sub><i>-</i>templated carbon carbonized at 900 °C; long-term cycling performance of sodium-ion batteries with templated carbons as anode materials; Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion coefficients from GITT for desodiation during cycling of sodium-ion batteries with templated carbons as anode materials (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/suppl_file/cm3c01520_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520/suppl_file/cm3c01520_si_001.pdf">cm3c01520_si_001.pdf (2.24 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acs.chemmater.3c01520" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

Ed.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2004</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>43</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">5785</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">5789</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/anie.200461051</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D43%26publication_year%3D2004%26pages%3D5785-5789%26journal%3DAngew.%2BChem.%2BInt.%2BEd.%26author%3DC.%2BLiang%26author%3DK.%2BHong%26author%3DG.%2BA.%2BGuiochon%26author%3DJ.%2BW.%2BMays%26author%3DS.%2BDai%26title%3DSynthesis%2Bof%2Ba%2Blarge-scale%2Bhighly%2Bordered%2Bporous%2Bcarbon%2Bfilm%2Bby%2Bself-assembly%2Bof%2Bblock%2Bcopolymers%26doi%3D10.1002%252Fanie.200461051&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.chemmater.3c01520&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Fanie.200461051&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Nanoporous films: Synthesis of a large-scale highly ordered porous carbon film by self-assembly of block copolymers</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liang, Chengdu; Hong, Kunlun; Guiochon, Georges A.; Mays, Jimmy W.; Dai, Sheng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Angewandte Chemie, International Edition</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2004</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">43</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">43</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5785-5789</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ACIEF5</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1433-7851</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The synthesis of well-defined porous carbon films involves four steps: 1) monomer-block copolymer film casting, 2) structure refining through solvent annealing, 3) polymn. of the carbon precursor, and 4) carbonization.                         cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Templated Nanocarbons for Energy Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Nishihara, Hirotomo; Kyotani, Takashi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The template carbonization method is a powerful tool for producing carbon materials with precisely controlled structures at the nanometer level.  The resulting templated nanocarbons exhibit extraordinarily unique (often ordered) structures that could never be produced by any of the conventional methods for carbon prodn.  This review summarizes recent publications about templated nanocarbons and their composites used for energy storage applications, including hydrogen storage, electrochem. capacitors, and lithium-ion batteries.  The main objective of this review is to clarify the true significance of the templated nanocarbons for each application.  For this purpose, the performance characteristics of almost all templated nanocarbons reported thus far are listed and compared with those of conventional materials, so that the advantages/disadvantages of the templated nanocarbons are elucidated.  From the practical point of view, the high prodn. cost and poor mass-producibility of the templated nanocarbons make them rather difficult to utilize; however, the study of their unique, specific, and ordered structures enables a deeper insight into energy storage mechanisms and the guidelines for developing energy storage materials.  Thus, another important purpose of this work is to establish such general guidelines and to propose future strategies for the prodn. of carbon materials with improved performance for energy storage applications.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo8CDR-XCroM7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljEvHNFA4Ehmg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XhtVehs7zE&amp;md5=90342c4e122bceee10de84abf383da96</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1016/S1872-5805(22)60575-4"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hong, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xie, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gu, D.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Recent progress of mesoporous carbons applied in electrochemical catalysis</span>. <i>New Carbon Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2022</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>37</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">152</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">179</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  An ideal adsorbent should possess the ability of being easily fabricated, cheap to produce, and have a high adsorptive capacity for adsorbates.  Ordered mesoporous carbon (OMC), a moderately new member of the carbonaceous family of adsorbents, has arisen as a potential adsorbent because of its distinct features such as high BET surface area, large pore size and porosity vol., tunable surface chem. and thermo-mech. stability.  In this review paper, we have presented a systematic documentation of all the research efforts on OMC prepn., modification, characterization, and environmental application for pollutant removal. The following study is based on very recent works on the meso-organization of thin SiO2 films using tetraethylorthosilicate (TEOS) as the inorg. source and cetyltrimethylammonium bromide (CTAB) as the structuring agent, but the method can also be extended to other systems based on nonsilica oxides and block copolymer surfactants.  The organization depends mainly on the chem. compn. of the film when it reaches the modulable steady state (MSS), where the inorg. framework is still flexible and the compn. is stable after reaching an equil. in the diffusion of volatile species.         </div><div class="casAbstract">C porous materials with a periodically ordered pore structure, controlled pore size and geometry and high thermal stability are synthesized using self-assembly of environmentally friendly phloroglucinol/glyoxylic acid precursors with an amphiphilic triblock copolymer template.  Glyoxylic acid, a plant-derived compd., is used for the 1st time as a substituent of carcinogen formaldehyde usually employed in such a synthesis.  Thanks to the double functionality, i.e., aldehyde and carboxylic acid, glyoxylic acid plays not only the role of a cross-linker for the formation of the resin but also the role of a catalyst by creation of H-bonding or specific reactions between the precursors.  Hence, no extra catalyst such as strong acids (HCl) or bases (NaOH) is any longer required.  C films and powders were successfully prepd. with high surface areas (up to 800 m2 g-1), high porous vol. (up to 1 cm3 g-1), tunable pore size (0.6 nm to 7 nm) and various pore architectures (hexagonal, cubic, and ink-bottle) by tuning the precursor ratio and by applying different manufg. engineering strategies.  Insights on the synthesis mechanism of the phenolic resin and C meso-structures were obtained using several anal. techniques, i.e., NMR (13C NMR) and FTIR spectroscopy, temp. programmed desorption coupled with mass spectrometry (TPD-MS) and thermo-gravimetric anal. (TGA).</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGouHqblL7AP47Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lg98ePXV8NJyg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXos1Cisrs%253D&amp;md5=24a5d3aa2e0e68fad67e655f6835b277</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1039/c2sm07448f"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chuenchom, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kraehnert, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Smarsly, B.                         cas_cit8R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">8</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Recent progress in soft-templating of porous carbon materials</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Chuenchom, Laemthong; Kraehnert, Ralph; Smarsly, Bernd M.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Soft Matter</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Mesoporous carbons synthesized via a soft-templating approach have attracted much attention due to their easy synthesis and facile control over the derived pore structure.  In analogy to soft-templating approaches for mesoporous metal oxides, their synthesis is based on a sequence of forming supramol. arrangements of precursor mols. with the soft templates, stabilization of the precursor framework by polymn. and finally the removal of the templates.  Using micelles of amphiphilic block-copolymers as templates, facile control over the morphol. and size of mesopores can be achieved by e.g. controlling size, compn., and concn. of the template polymers or compn. and d.p. of the precursor.  Moreover, soft templating approaches can be extended to obtain also carbon materials with hierarchical meso- and macroporosity.  The addnl. macroporosity either can result from templating by polymer latex or is induced via macrophase sepn.  In this review, we describe recent progress and examples in the synthesis and application of mesoporous carbon materials based on soft-templating approaches.  Moreover, we reiterate fundamental principles of self-aggregation, highlight proposed synthesis mechanisms and present means of controlling pore size, also in hierarchical meso-macroporous carbon materials.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpo4M2vKKM7oLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lg98ePXV8NJyg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XhsV2qsLrM&amp;md5=6938a58316e10598f600e2593741470a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1039/C8GC03497D"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Herou, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ribadeneyra, M.                         cas_cit9R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">9</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Ordered mesoporous carbons from lignin: a new class of biobased electrodes for supercapacitors</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Herou, Servann; Ribadeneyra, Maria Crespo; Madhu, Rajesh; Araullo-Peters, Vicente; Jensen, Anders; Schlee, Philipp; Titirici, Magdalena</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Green Chemistry</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">We report the synthesis of sustainable ordered mesoporous carbons (OMCs) produced from lignin, using the evapn. induced self-assembly (EISA) method.  We demonstrated that it is possible to replace half of the phloroglucinol (a well-known carbon precursor currently not derived from bioprecursors) by hardwood organosolv lignin while obtaining a highly ordered pore structure. Herein, a feasible method for the prepn. of lignin-based carbon nanotubes (-CNTs) using lignin as the carbon source based on mobile chem. vapor deposition is proposed.  First, lignin-based micelles were used as a soft template to prep. a catalyst, and then -CNTs were synthesized from gaseous products generated by lignin pyrolysis.                 10.1021/acs.chemmater.5b04407</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D28%26publication_year%3D2016%26pages%3D1653-1667%26journal%3DChem.%2BMater.%26author%3DH.%2BN.%2BLokupitiya%26author%3DA.%2BJones%26author%3DB.%2BReid%26author%3DS.%2BGuldin%26author%3DM.%2BStefik%26title%3DOrdered%2BMesoporous%2Bto%2BMacroporous%2BOxides%2Bwith%2BTunable%2BIsomorphic%2BArchitectures%253A%2BSolution%2BCriteria%2Bfor%2BPersistent%2BMicelle%2BTemplates%26doi%3D10.1021%252Facs.chemmater.5b04407&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.chemmater.3c01520&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Facs.chemmater.5b04407&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Ordered Mesoporous to Macroporous Oxides with Tunable Isomorphic Architectures: Solution Criteria for Persistent Micelle Templates</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Lokupitiya, Hasala N.; Jones, Alexis; Reid, Barry; Guldin, Stefan; Stefik, Morgan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">28</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">6</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1653-1667</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CMATEX</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0897-4756</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Porous and nanoscale architectures of inorg. materials have become crucial for a range of energy and catalysis applications, where the ability to control the morphol. largely dets. the transport characteristics and device performance.  Despite the availability of a range of block copolymer self-assembly methods, the conditions for tuning the key architectural features such as the inorg. wall-thickness have remained elusive.  Toward this end, we have developed soln. processing guidelines that enable isomorphic nanostructures with tunable wall-thickness.  A new poly(ethylene oxide-b-hexyl acrylate) (PEO-b-PHA) structure-directing agent (SDA) was used to demonstrate the key soln. design criteria.  Specifically, the use of a polymer with a high Flory-Huggins effective interaction parameter, χ, and appropriate soln. conditions leads to the kinetic entrapment of persistent micelle templates (PMT) for tunable isomorphic architectures.  Soly. parameters are used to predict conditions for maintaining persistent micelle sizes despite changing equil. conditions.  Here, the use of different inorg. loadings controls the inorg. wall-thickness with const. pore size.  This versatile method enabled a record 55 nm oxide wall-thickness from micelle coassembly as well as the seamless transition from mesoporous materials to macroporous materials by varying the polymer molar mass and soln. conditions.  The processing guidelines are generalizable and were elaborated with three inorg. systems, including Nb2O5, WO3, and SiO2, that were thermally stable to 600 °C for access to cryst. materials.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpNanclANmqj7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgrebREG0FJuQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XitF2nsr8%253D&amp;md5=8bd7fda614763c13110587cce5565873</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit15" data-doi="10.1016/j.carbon.2017.02.016"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Libbrecht, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">an Verberckmoes</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Thybaut, J.                 10.1016/j.carbon.2017.02.016</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D116%26publication_year%3D2017%26pages%3D528-546%26journal%3DCarbon%26author%3DW.%2BLibbrecht%26author%3D%2Ban%2BVerberckmoes%26author%3DJ.%2BW.%2BThybaut%26author%3DP.%2Bvan%2Bder%2BVoort%26author%3DJ.%2Bde.%2BClercq%26title%3DSoft%2Btemplated%2Bmesoporous%2Bcarbons%253A%2BTuning%2Bthe%2Bporosity%2Bfor%2Bthe%2Badsorption%2Bof%2Blarge%2Borganic%2Bpollutants%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.carbon.2017.02.016&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.chemmater.3c01520&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.carbon.2017.02.016&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit15R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">15</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Soft templated mesoporous carbons: Tuning the porosity for the adsorption of large organic pollutants</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Libbrecht, Wannes; Verberckmoes, An; Thybaut, Joris W.; Van Der Voort, Pascal; De Clercq, Jeriffa</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Carbon</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">116</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">528-546</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CRBNAH</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0008-6223</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review concerning the synthesis of soft-templated mesoporous carbons focusing on their porosity-tuning, pore size control, and potential as a new family of C adsorbents, is given.  Fundamental studies revealed numerous synthesis routes which can adjust material properties: sp. surface area, pore vol., pore size or morphol., and elemental compn.  An extensive collection of template materials exist which can be impregnated with C precursors to provide various hard-templated mesoporous carbons with a precise control of mesoporosity and pore geometry.  The more recent direct method or soft template method offers specific advantages.  Using org.-org. self-assembly between polymerizable precursors and block copolymer surfactants as structure-directing agents, it is possible to obtain tunable morphol. and surface properties, mech. stability, and reduce the no. of synthesis steps, thereby decreasing synthesis cost and complexity.  Fine-tuning material properties or possible post-modification or nanoparticle doping result in applications in catalysis, electrochem., energy storage, sepn., or adsorption.  Mesoporous carbons are considered a new family of adsorbents challenging well-known activated C by introducing the advantage of increased pore size which allows mesoporous carbons to adsorb larger mols. and enhance adsorption kinetics.  Topics discussed include: introduction, mesoporous C synthesis (precursor types, synthesis pathways and type of catalysis, surfactant types, precursor:surfactant ratio, carbonization temp., surface modification); org. pollutant adsorption (bisphenol A soln., org. dyes, lysozyme, humic acid); and conclusions.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpxWOTSJ_5vILVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgrebREG0FJuQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXivFarsr4%253D&amp;md5=2fa477648fe4a3b59da45b95e65eccc3</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit16" data-doi="10.3390/c9010027"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Osswald, O.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Loeh, M.                         cas_cit17R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">17</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Assessing the structural properties of graphitic and non-graphitic carbons by Raman spectroscopy</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Schuepfer, Dominique B.; Badaczewski, Felix; Guerra-Castro, Juan Manuel; Hofmann, Detlev M.; Heiliger, Christian; Smarsly, Bernd; Klar, Peter J.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Carbon</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">161</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">359-372</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CRBNAH</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0008-6223</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">We study the transformation from mol. to cryst. of (non-)graphitic carbons synthesized from org. precursors by heat treatment.  Easy assessment of structural properties resulting from heat treatment protocols is mandatory for industrial process monitoring.  Raman spectroscopy, in particular, the Raman lineshape anal. of G and D mode, offers quick assessment of the av. sheet size of such carbons.  We validate this method by performing Raman, WAXS, and EPR measurements of series of resin and pitch-based carbons synthesized.  The crystallite sizes of the WAXS anal. for the individual samples are related to corresponding positions and linewidths of G and D Raman modes and show excellent agreement between expt. and modeling from large sizes down to 4 nm.  The theor. master curves are independent of the precursor used in the synthesis, in contrast to models for the intensity ratio of D and G band vs. size.  The latter are not universally valid and differ for each class of precursors.  For sizes &lt;4 nm, our lineshape model fails as it is based on the band structure and phonon dispersions of ideal graphene.  Thus, 4 nm corresponds to the fundamental transition from mol. to cryst. character for non-graphitic carbons.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpwk2Ph-RL_C7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgrebREG0FJuQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXit1OksLo%253D&amp;md5=592d97fe66b623f3f6d2244da314e7ca</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit18" data-doi="10.1016/j.carbon.2018.09.025"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Badaczewski, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Loeh, M.                 10.1016/j.carbon.2018.09.025</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D141%26publication_year%3D2019%26pages%3D169-181%26journal%3DCarbon%26author%3DF.%2BBadaczewski%26author%3DM.%2BO.%2BLoeh%26author%3DT.%2BPfaff%26author%3DS.%2BDobrotka%26author%3DD.%2BWallacher%26author%3DD.%2BClemens%26author%3DJ.%2BMetz%26author%3DB.%2BM.%2BSmarsly%26title%3DPeering%2Binto%2Bthe%2Bstructural%2Bevolution%2Bof%2Bglass-like%2Bcarbons%2Bderived%2Bfrom%2Bphenolic%2Bresin%2Bby%2Bcombining%2Bsmall-angle%2Bneutron%2Bscattering%2Bwith%2Ban%2Badvanced%2Bevaluation%2Bmethod%2Bfor%2Bwide-angle%2BX-ray%2Bscattering%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.carbon.2018.09.025&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.chemmater.3c01520&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.carbon.2018.09.025&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit18R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">18</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Peering into the structural evolution of glass-like carbons derived from phenolic resin by combining small-angle neutron scattering with an advanced evaluation method for wide-angle X-ray scattering</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Badaczewski, F.; Loeh, M. O.; Pfaff, T.; Dobrotka, S.; Wallacher, D.; Clemens, D.; Metz, J.; Smarsly, B. M.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Carbon</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">141</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">169-181</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CRBNAH</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0008-6223</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The structural evolution of 2 non-graphitizing glass-like carbons derived from a liq. resole and a solid novolac-type phenolic resin was quant. characterized by combining small-angle neutron scattering (SANS) with an advanced evaluation for wide-angle X-ray scattering (WAXS) data.  Utilizing these 2 methods allowed for studying the microstructure on the angstrom level (graphene stacks, WAXS) and the inaccessible microporosity (SANS).  The applied WAXS anal. provided quant. structural parameters for both size and disorder in the polyarom. sp2 microstructure.  Hence, the combined SANS-WAXS anal. yielded comprehensive insights into the relation between the graphene microstructure and the inaccessible porosity upon heat treatment for glass-like carbons, i.e. a non-graphitizing class of carbon.  In particular, the analog investigation of a graphitizing mesophase pitch demonstrates the major impact of the chem. compn. of the utilized carbon precursor.  For the glass-like carbons the results revealed different growth rates for the lateral extent of the basic structural units (La) depending on the temp. range, finally reaching 12 nm, whereas the stack height (Lc) exhibiting 2.2 nm is hardly affected by the thermal processing ≤3000°.  As a major finding our study thus relates the evolution of microstructure and porosity to changes in chem. compn.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrkLtbYRlphJrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljdiO9CxUPwHw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXhvVSntrfI&amp;md5=6e0a30d827b00e417d349b66719e0a10</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref19"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">19</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit19" data-doi="10.1002/zaac.201400210"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Faber, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Badaczewski, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ruland, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Smarsly, B.                         cas_cit19R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">19</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Investigation of the Microstructure of Disordered, Non--graphitic Carbons by an Advanced Analysis Method for Wide-Angle X-ray Scattering</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Faber, Kristin; Badaczewski, Felix; Ruland, Wilhelm; Smarsly, Bernd M.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Zeitschrift fuer Anorganische und Allgemeine Chemie</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">640</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">15</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3107-3117</span>CODEN:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The anal. of the wide-angle X-ray scattering (WAXS) data of non-graphitic carbons is challenging due to the broad and overlapped (hk)- and (001)-reflections.  A recently developed advanced anal. method developed by Ruland and Smarsly allows for the fitting of the WAXS data by a continuous model function, providing a detailed insight into the sp2-carbon microstructure.  Obtaining parameters like the graphene layer extent La, the av. stack height Lc and disorder effects permits the quantification of microstructural features.  Firstly, this publication gives a brief review over historical und recent developments regarding the evaluation of the WAXS of disordered carbons.  Secondly, the applicability of this algorithm to carbon samples possessing a substantial degree of disorder is tested by investigating activated carbons, heat treated coal tar pitch and furfuryl alc.  It is seen that several structural parameters are detd. with reasonable accuracy and thereby the influence of the precursor and the temp. on the resulting carbon microstructure is identified.  The decrease in disorder within the 2D graphene lattice and in the stacking of the layers at higher synthesis temps. is quantified.  Moreover, this study reveals that the graphene layer extent La is only about a few nanometers in size, showing that typical non-graphitic carbons used for diverse applications possess quite small dimensions of graphene stacks.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp2c_wn-WaDJrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljdiO9CxUPwHw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXht1aksrbL&amp;md5=0fe962bb56cbdc1a8c21732036f50752</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref20"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">20</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit20" data-doi="10.1107/S0021889802011007"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ruland, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Smarsly, B.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">X-ray scattering of non-graphitic carbon: an improved method of evaluation</span>. <i>J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The wide-angle X-ray scattering (WAXS) of non-graphitic carbons shows relatively broad and diffuse interference maxima.  In most cases, the corresponding line profiles cannot be sepd. unambiguously, so that an evaluation using profile anal. is difficult.  In these cases, fitting procedures for the total scattering curve are more appropriate.  This requires theor. expressions for all interference maxima with a sufficient no. of relevant structural parameters to ensure an acceptable fit.  In this paper, such expressions are presented for the (hk) lines (intralayer scattering) and the (00l) lines (interlayer scattering), and the application of these expressions to the evaluation of selected WAXS curves is described.  Furthermore, the effects of thermal motion, preferred orientation and Compton scattering are discussed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpmS6e7z2JIyrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljdiO9CxUPwHw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD38Xms1yrtL4%253D&amp;md5=66580502fc8b25cb26cff436f9ed61e4</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref21"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">21</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit21" data-doi="10.3390/c8040078"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Osswald, O.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Smarsly, B.                         cas_cit22R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">22</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A revised mechanistic model for sodium insertion in hard carbons</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Au, Heather; Alptekin, Hande; Jensen, Anders C. S.; Olsson, Emilia; O'Keefe, Christopher A.; Smith, Thomas; Crespo-Ribadeneyra, Maria; Headen, Thomas F.; Grey, Clare P.; Cai, Qiong; Drew, Alan J.; Titirici, Maria-Magdalena</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbons have shown considerable promise as anodes for emerging sodium-ion battery technologies.  Current understanding of sodium-storage behavior in hard carbons attributes capacity to filling of graphitic interlayers and pores, and adsorption at defects, although there is still considerable debate regarding the voltages at which these mechanisms occur.  Here, ex situ23Na solid-state NMR and total scattering studies on a systematically tuned series of hard carbons revealed the formation of increasingly metallic sodium clusters in direct correlation to the growing pore size, occurring only in samples which exhibited a low voltage plateau.  Combining exptl. results with DFT calcns., we propose a revised mechanistic model in which sodium ions store first simultaneously and continuously at defects, within interlayers and on pore surfaces.  Once these higher energy binding sites are filled, pore filling occurs during the plateau region, where the densely confined sodium takes on a greater degree of metallicity.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoSrEjXCn1VBrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljdiO9CxUPwHw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXhsFyktLjJ&amp;md5=a7181f3c171e70a094942fc79cb26548</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref23"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">23</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit23" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201903176"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Morikawa, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nishimura, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hashimoto, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ohnuma, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yamada, A.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Mechanism of Sodium Storage in Hard Carbon: An X-Ray Scattering Analysis</span>. <i>Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbon is a std. anode material for Na-ion batteries.  However, its low crystallinity and diverse microstructures make obtaining a full understanding of the sodium storage mechanism challenging.  Here, the results of a systematic ex situ small and wide angle X-ray scattering study of a series of nanostructured hard carbons, which reveal clear evidence of sodium storage in the graphene-graphene interlayers and nanopores, are presented.  Particularly, an emergence of a broad peak around q ≈ 2.0-2.1 Å-1 in the low voltage region is suggested to be an indicator that sodium is densely confined in the nanopores.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The increasing interest in nanostructured porous carbon materials in the field of energy storage and electrocatalysis has led to drastic improvement in their properties.  These materials are still expensive to manuf., often due to the harsh chem. treatments required to enhance porosity.  In this article, we demonstrate that the process of activating carbons does not uniquely depend on the amt. of activating agent (NaOH) but also on the contact area between the salt and the carbonaceous framework to be activated.  We compare the microstructure, double layer capacitance and microporosity of carbon materials of the same chem. compn. but obtained through two different protocols: electrospinning, which allows the formation of small NaOH domains (~ 60 nm) homogeneously distributed across lignin fibers, and std. activation, which leads to the growth of larger NaOH domains within the lignin framework.  As intimate contact between both phases ensures max. activation, our electrospun material shows a remarkable capacitance (~ 180 F g-1) in comparison to the material prepd. through std. "mix and dry" activation procedure (~ 10 F g-1).  This is attributed to the generation of micropores (~ 670 m2 g-1), tailored from the small activating salt template domains in intimate contact with the lignin framework.  In summary, through this work we establish a relationship between the activating agent's morphol. and its effect in porosity and energy storage performance.  We present a facile method to interpenetrate the chem. activating agent within the carbon precursor structure before pyrolysis, through the efficient usage of the activating agent.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq28G0eNKMGT7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liUIKOLemTXuQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXkvFGjtg%253D%253D&amp;md5=a1cdf7358d237ed4a266ba155197489b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref25"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">25</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit25" data-doi="10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b01969"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bommier, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Surta, T.                         cas_cit25R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">25</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">New Mechanistic Insights on Na-Ion Storage in Nongraphitizable Carbon</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Bommier, Clement; Surta, Todd Wesley; Dolgos, Michelle; Ji, Xiulei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
