        </div><div class="casAbstract">Nongraphitizable carbon, also known as hard carbon, is considered one of the most promising anodes for the emerging Na-ion batteries.  The current mechanistic understanding of Na-ion storage in hard carbon is based on the "card-house" model first raised in the early 2000s.  This model describes that Na-ion insertion occurs first through intercalation between graphene sheets in turbostratic nanodomains, followed by Na filling of the pores in the carbon structure.  We tried to test this model by tuning the sizes of turbostratic nanodomains but revealed a correlation between the structural defects and Na-ion storage.  Based on our exptl. data, we propose an alternative perspective for sodiation of hard carbon that consists of Na-ion storage at defect sites, by intercalation and last via pore-filling.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqegbEbsDPzrrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liUIKOLemTXuQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXht1Oqsb3M&amp;md5=a2144d9fa8c9f19a479ebec0ec74f1eb</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref26"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">26</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit26" data-doi="10.1039/c3cp55072a"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Stoeckel, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wallacher, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zickler, G.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Characterization of mesoporous adsorbents is traditionally performed in terms of the pore size distribution with bulk methods like physisorption and mercury intrusion.  Their application relies on assumptions regarding the basic pore geometry.  Although novel tools have enabled the quant. interpretation of physisorption data for adsorbents having a well-defined pore structure, the anal. of disordered mesoporosity still remains challenging.  The authors show that small angle X-ray scattering (SAXS) combined with chord length distribution (CLD) anal. presents a precise and convenient approach to det. the structural properties of two-phase (solid-void) systems of mesopores.  Characteristic wall (solid) and pore (void) sizes as well as surface areas are extd. without the need to assume a certain pore shape. Mean pore size and surface area are compared with results obtained from nitrogen physisorption data and show excellent agreement.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqMvAin3ntHZ7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liUIKOLemTXuQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXktV2is7c%253D&amp;md5=18ba0bfc72e4757364051f2b57ef03f8</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref27"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">27</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit27" data-doi="10.1039/C4RA14246B"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ghimbeu, C.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The synthesis of ordered porous carbons by a green, simple and rapid approach based on light exposure of a soln. of carbon precursors and a soft-template in the presence or not of a photosensitizer is demonstrated herein along with their formation mechanism.                         cas_cit29R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">29</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Direct synthesis of ordered mesoporous carbons</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ma, Tian-Yi; Liu, Lei; Yuan, Zhong-Yong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Ordered mesoporous carbon materials have recently aroused great research interest because of their widespread applications in many areas such as adsorbents, catalysts and supports, gas storage hosts, and electrode materials.  The direct prepn. strategy from org.-org. self-assembly involving the combination of polymerizable precursors and block copolymer templates is expected to be more flexible in prepg. mesoporous carbons, compared with the traditional nanocasting strategy of complicated and high-cost procedures using mesoporous silica materials as the hard template.  In this review, the fundamentals and recent advances related to the field of ordered mesoporous carbon materials from the direct prepn. strategy of block copolymer soft-templating are presented, with a focus on their controllable prepn., modification, and potential applications.  Under the guidance of their formation mechanism, the prepn. of ordered mesoporous carbons are discussed in detail by consulting different exptl. conditions, including prepn. pathways, precursors, catalysts, and templates.  Both the mesopore size and morphol. control are introduced.  The potential applications of pure mesoporous carbons, nonmetallic- and metallic-modified mesoporous carbons, and some interpenetrating carbon-based composites are demonstrated.                         cas_cit30R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">30</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Expanded Kinetic Control for Persistent Micelle Templates with Solvent Selection</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Sarkar, Amrita; Evans, Laurel; Stefik, Morgan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Langmuir</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The precision control of nanoscale materials remains a challenge for the study of nanostructure-performance relationships.  Persistent micelle templates (PMT) are a kinetic-controlled self-assembly approach that decouples pore and wall control.  Here, block copolymer surfactants form persistent micelles that maintain const. template size as material precursors are added, despite the shifting equil. dimensions.  Earlier PMT demonstrations were based upon solvent mixts. where kinetic rates were adjusted with the amt. of water cosolvent.  This approach is however limited because ever-higher water contents can lead to secondary porosity within the material walls.  Herein, we report an improved method to regulate the PMT kinetics via the majority solvent.  This enables a new avenue for the expansion of the PMT window to realize templated materials with a greater extent of tunability.  In addn., we report a new small-angle X-ray scattering (SAXS)-based log-log anal. method to independently test the micelle-templated series for consistency with the expected lattice expansion with an increasing material:template ratio.  The PMT window identified by the log-log anal. of the SAXS data agreed well with independent SEM measurements.  The combination of improved micelle control with solvent selection along with SAXS validation will accelerate the development of a myriad of nanomaterial applications.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpqHe83Sn4WSrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj02UBiap80sw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXosVektr8%253D&amp;md5=837d89c909c830fdaab01e2c04b2ce04</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref31"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">31</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit31" data-doi="10.1039/C7TA01034F"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sarkar, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Stefik, M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">How to make persistent micelle templates in 24 h and know it using X-ray scattering</span>. <i>J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The controlled fabrication of nanoscale materials can enable new behaviors and properties as well as improved performance.  For example, many electrochem. devices are made from porous materials where the architecture of both the porosity and the material each affect distinct processes.  Recently, persistent micelle templating (PMT) emerged as a unique nanofabrication technique that enables decoupled control over the porosity and wall material dimensions via self-assembly.  PMT control relies upon kinetic entrapment to preserve the micelle diam. while adding material.  However, the development of PMT is currently cumbersome where time-intensive polymns. and soln. parameter searches are both required.  Here we report simple SAXS based geometric models that significantly expedite the identification of the PMT window with a one-pot titrn.-approach.  The models also quant. predict the nominal template diam. and wall-thickness within the PMT window.  This approach yielded the first PMT criteria for a low molar mass block copolymer with ∼13 nm mesopores and continuously tunable wall-thickness with 2 Å increments.  Furthermore, we demonstrate an accelerated synthesis that includes custom polymer fabrication and micelle templating within 24 h.  The polymer synthesis was demonstrated without high-vacuum equipment and only used low-cost, com. available reagents.  These advances will ease and accelerate the use of PMT for a wide gamut of nanomaterials investigations.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqfVxDq_OQ_r7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj02UBiap80sw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXmtVGiurk%253D&amp;md5=fc735d54caabf8bcf4a07ccf3119c6b1</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref32"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">32</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit32" data-doi="10.1103/PhysRev.110.1"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Percus, J.                         cas_cit34R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">34</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Characterization of Hierarchically Ordered Porous Materials by Physisorption and Mercury Porosimetry-A Tutorial Review</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Schlumberger, Carola; Thommes, Matthias</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials Interfaces</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  This paper is devoted to the textural characterization of nanoporous materials with a focus on hierarchically ordered materials such as mesoporous zeolites, which exhibit an interconnected pore network consisting of micro-, meso-, and often macropores.  Hierarchically ordered zeolites have the potential to improve various industrial applications for instance in the areas of heterogeneous catalysis, sepn., gas, and energy storage.  Detailed insights into the pore architecture are important, because they control transport phenomena, diffusional rates, and govern selectivity, e.g. in catalyzed reactions.  However, a reliable characterization of such complex pore structures is still a major challenge.  Within this context, the application of advanced physisorption methodologies for micro-mesopore anal. is discussed but also the characterization of macroporosity by mercury porosimetry is addressed.  Fundamental concepts and recent major advances in understanding the underlying mechanisms are highlighted.  In conjunction with selected case studies, it is illustrated how the application of advanced physisorption methodologies allows i. for the detn. of reliable surface areas, pore vols., and pore size distributions and ii. for obtaining information about pore network characteristics.  This tutorial offers guidance for an advanced characterization of nanoporous materials by physisorption and mercury intrusion/extrusion.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpYsH6GAHMLv7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj70nM6P6vY8w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3MXks1CisLc%253D&amp;md5=8dc45eb1211539df9652cb840505474a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref35"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">35</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit35" data-doi="10.1021/la0340807"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Thomas, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Schlaad, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Smarsly, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Antonietti, M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Replication of Lyotropic Block Copolymer Mesophases into Porous Silica by Nanocasting: Learning about Finer Details of Polymer Self-Assembly</span>. <i>Langmuir</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2003</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>19</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">4455</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">4459</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Block copolymers, easily tailor-made by growing poly(ethylene oxide) from a com. hydroxyl-terminated poly(ethylene-co-butylene) (Kraton liq.), can self-assemble over a wide range of concns. into mesophases constructed from spherical micelles with small polydispersity.                         cas_cit36R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">36</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">SANS Investigation of Nitrogen Sorption in Porous Silica</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Smarsly, Bernd; Goeltner, Christine; Antonietti, Markus; Ruland, Wilhelm; Hoinkis, Ernst</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry B</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2001</span>),

        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">4</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The mechanism of nitrogen sorption in porous silica was investigated by small-angle neutron scattering (SANS).  Two samples of porous silica were studied contg. mesopores (pore sizes 5.5 and 9.5 nm, resp.) and addnl. micropores of irregular shape and statistical distribution.  SANS curves were recorded at a temp. of 78 K at various relative pressures p/p0 during adsorption.  The expt. is based on contrast matching between silica and condensed nitrogen with regard to neutron scattering.  The sorption process was characterized by the evaluation of the chord-length distributions extd. from SANS data for each p/p0.  In addn., a general approach was developed to relate the SANS pattern during capillary condensation to the size distribution and the morphol. of ordered mesopores.  On the basis of these evaluation methods, various uptake mechanisms could be described, which are micropore filling, the formation of nitrogen layers, and capillary condensation.  The anal. of the SANS data shows that the mean size of the remaining empty mesopores formally increases, and their size distribution becomes narrower during capillary condensation, which is in agreement with the predictions of the Kelvin equation.  Furthermore, the study indicates a significant degree of addnl. microporosity, the origin of which is discussed.  For comparison, the expt. and the data evaluation were also applied to a disordered porous silica with a broad pore size distribution.  The combination of SANS and nitrogen sorption turned out to be a powerful technique to investigate both the mechanisms of sorption and the structure of porous silicas in one expt.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqPodWi2okONrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj70nM6P6vY8w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD3MXms1Wr&amp;md5=91029704b2a6220c4ed43f28f8d8bc40</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref37"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">37</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit37" data-doi="10.1103/PhysRevLett.10.321"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wertheim, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The combination of physisorption expts. with simultaneous in situ small-angle x-ray and neutron scattering (SAXS/SANS) was used to elucidate the porosity in mesoporous silica with a trimodal pore structure.  The material (KLE-IL) contains spherical mesopores of 14 nm in diam., worm-like mesopores (2-3 nm), and micropores, templated by a block copolymer and an ionic liq. surfactant, while the micropores originate from the hydrophilic block of the block copolymer.  The main objective of the study was the quantification of the microporosity and the small mesopores and to find out if they are indeed located between the larger, spherical mesopores.  The in situ SAXS/SANS expts. took advantage of contrast matching of nitrogen (SANS, T = 77 K) and dibromomethane (SAXS, T = 290 K).  By using the latter gas with a slightly larger kinetic diam., it was possible to judge the accessibility of the pores under ambient conditions.  The in situ expts. were supported by high-precision ex situ physisorption.                         cas_cit41R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">41</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Temperature-regulated biomass-derived hard carbon as a superior anode for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Rong-Rong; He, Xiang-Xi; Yang, Zhuo; Liu, Xiao-Hao; Qiao, Yun; Xu, Li; Li, Li; Chou, Shu-Lei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Materials Chemistry Frontiers</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbon is considered as one of the most promising carbonaceous anode materials to promote the commercialization of sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).  However, the low initial coulombic efficiency (ICE) of hard carbon materials and the mechanism of sodium storage are still controversial, which seriously restricts their practical applications in SIBs.  Herein, hard carbon materials with a multichannel structure derived from golden berry leaves were successfully prepd. via a simple carbonization method that was carried out in the temp. range of 1000°C to 2000°C.  The initial discharge capacity of the sample carbonized at 1400°C can reach 338.7 mA h g-1 at 20 mA g-1, and deliver an excellent ICE of 86.43%.  Furthermore, when the carbonization temp. reaches 2000°C, the contribution of the plateau-specific capacity can reach up to 91.11%.  Combining both exptl. characterization results and anal., the mechanism of sodium storage in hard carbon can be considered as the slope capacity corresponding to the adsorption of sodium ions on defect sites and heteroatoms, while the plateau capacity corresponds to the sodium ion intercalation into the interlayers and filling in the micropores.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrIak_GcOPBNrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhLyY70qrVdRQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3MXhvVyjtr3K&amp;md5=b1d902b4d809affd4838b65c54c10c95</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref42"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">42</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit42" data-doi="10.1038/nnano.2013.46"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ferrari, A.                         cas_cit42R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">42</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Raman spectroscopy as a versatile tool for studying the properties of graphene</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ferrari, Andrea C.; Basko, Denis M.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Nanotechnology</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">8</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Raman spectroscopy is an integral part of graphene research.  It is used to det. the no. and orientation of layers, the quality and types of edge, and the effects of perturbations, such as elec. and magnetic fields, strain, doping, disorder and functional groups.  This, in turn, provides insight into all sp2-bonded carbon allotropes, because graphene is their fundamental building block.  Here we review the state of the art, future directions and open questions in Raman spectroscopy of graphene.  We describe essential phys. processes whose importance has only recently been recognized, such as the various types of resonance at play, and the role of quantum interference.  We update all basic concepts and notations, and propose a terminol. that is able to describe any result in literature.  We finally highlight the potential of Raman spectroscopy for layered materials other than graphene.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqOpvJ2PMi3aLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhLyY70qrVdRQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXltFCktLY%253D&amp;md5=3ff9fb11a3ea9c30781b3e2ada00f82e</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref43"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">43</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit43" data-doi="10.1016/j.carbon.2005.02.018"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sadezky, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Muckenhuber, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Grothe, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Niessner, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pöschl, U.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Raman microspectroscopy of soot and related carbonaceous materials: Spectral analysis and structural information</span>. <i>Carbon</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2005</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>43</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1731</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">1742</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.carbon.2005.02.018</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D43%26publication_year%3D2005%26pages%3D1731-1742%26journal%3DCarbon%26author%3DA.%2BSadezky%26author%3DH.%2BMuckenhuber%26author%3DH.%2BGrothe%26author%3DR.%2BNiessner%26author%3DU.%2BP%25C3%25B6schl%26title%3DRaman%2Bmicrospectroscopy%2Bof%2Bsoot%2Band%2Brelated%2Bcarbonaceous%2Bmaterials%253A%2BSpectral%2Banalysis%2Band%2Bstructural%2Binformation%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.carbon.2005.02.018&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.chemmater.3c01520&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.carbon.2005.02.018&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit43R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">43</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Raman microspectroscopy of soot and related carbonaceous materials. Spectral analysis and structural information</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Sadezky, A.; Muckenhuber, H.; Grothe, H.; Niessner, R.; Poeschl, U.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Carbon</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2005</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">43</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Exptl. conditions and math. fitting procedures for the collection and anal. of Raman spectra of soot and related carbonaceous materials were investigated and optimized with a Raman microscope system operated at 3 different laser excitation wavelengths (514, 633, and 780 nm).  Several band combinations for spectral anal. were tested, and a combination of 4 Lorentzian-shaped bands (G, D1, D2, D4) at about 1580, 1350, 1620, and 1200 cm-1, resp., with a Gaussian-shaped band (D3) at ∼1500 cm-1 was best suited for the 1st-order spectra.  The 2nd-order spectra were best fitted with Lorentzian-shaped bands at about 2450, 2700, 2900, and 3100 cm-1.  Spectral parameters (band positions, full widths at half max., and intensity ratios) are reported for several types of industrial C black (Degussa Printex, Cabot Monarch), diesel soot (particulate matter from modern heavy duty vehicle and passenger car engine exhaust, NIST SRM1650), spark-discharge soot (Palas GfG100), and graphite.  Several parameters, in particular the width of the D1 band at ∼1350 cm-1, provide structural information and allow to discriminate the sample materials, but the characterization and distinction of different types of soot is limited by the exptl. reproducibility of the spectra and the statistical uncertainties of curve fitting.  The results are discussed and compared with x-ray diffraction measurements and earlier Raman spectroscopic studies of comparable materials, where different measurement and fitting procedures was applied.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqI47SxmqUfxrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhLyY70qrVdRQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD2MXltFGrtLc%253D&amp;md5=045e6d9ee2eb3d84bd001fca25bc7f62</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref44"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">44</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit44" data-doi="10.1063/1.4756995"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kaniyoor, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ramaprabhu, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Raman spectroscopic investigation of graphite oxide derived graphene</span>. <i>AIP Adv.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2012</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>2</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">032183</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit44R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">44</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A Raman spectroscopic investigation of graphite oxide derived graphene</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kaniyoor, Adarsh; Ramaprabhu, Sundara</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">AIP Advances</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">2</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Institute of Physics</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Graphene sheets that are now routinely obtained by the exfoliation/redn. of graphite oxide exhibit Raman spectra unlike traditional graphene systems.  The general attributes of the Raman spectra of these 'wrinkled graphene' are first reaffirmed by evaluating the spectra of samples prepd. by seven different exfoliation-redn. methods.  These graphene sheets exhibit highly broadened D and G Raman bands and in addn., have a modulated bump in place of the conventional 2D (G') band.  It is shown that the high wavenumber 'bump' can be resolved into the conventional 2D band and several defect activated peaks such as G*, D+D' and 2D'.  The broad G band could also be deconvoluted into the actual G band and the D' band, thereby attributing the broadening in the G band to the presence of this defect activated band.  Two addnl. modes, named as D* at 1190 cm-1 and D** at ∼1500 cm-1 could be identified.  These peculiar features in the Raman spectrum of 'graphene' are attributed to the highly disordered and wrinkled (defective) morphol. of the sheets.  The affect of defects are further augmented due to the finite crystallite size of these graphene sheets.  The dispersion in the band positions and peak intensities with respect to the laser energy are also demonstrated.                         cas_cit45R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">45</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">New lines in the Raman spectra of carbons and graphite</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Vidano, R.; Fischbach, D.     </div><div class="casAbstract">An extensive survey of the Raman spectral activity of a wide variety of carbon materials has produced exptl. evidence for at least 5 structure-sensitive lines or bands.  In addn. to the well-known, always present 1580 cm-1 graphite line and the 1360 cm-1 disordered C line, there is a disorder line at ≈1620 cm-1 that is responsible for the apparent blue-shift of the graphite line in very disordered C; and there are lines at ≈2700 and ≈2735 cm-1 that are strong in graphite and annealed C, but absent in very disordered C.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Graphene is the 2-dimensional building block for C allotropes of every other dimensionality.  Its electronic structure is captured in its Raman spectrum that clearly evolves with the no. of layers.  The D peak 2nd order changes in shape, width, and position for an increasing no. of layers, reflecting the change in the electron bands via a double resonant Raman process.  The G peak slightly down-shifts.  This allows unambiguous, high-throughput, nondestructive identification of graphene layers, which is critically lacking in this emerging research area.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo35CDycv0LILVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljCM-67xY3j3Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD28XhtFKqtbvP&amp;md5=8b1d9f77f616aea008d55ba4fbb3f0bb</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref47"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">47</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit47" data-doi="10.1063/1.1674108"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tuinstra, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Koenig, J.     </div><div class="casAbstract">Raman spectra are reported from single crystals of graphite and other graphite materials.  Single crystals of graphite show 1 single line at 1575 cm-1.  For the other materials like stress-annealed pyrolytic graphite, com. graphites, activated charcoal, lampblack, and vitreous C, another line is detected at 1355 cm-1.  The Raman intensity of this latter band is inversely proportional to the crystallite size and is caused by a breakdown of the k selection rule.  The intensity of this band allows an est. of the crystallite size in the surface layer of any C sample.  Two inplane force consts. are calcd. from the frequencies.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGr48A5_J6qsfrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljCM-67xY3j3Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADyaE3cXks1SqtLc%253D&amp;md5=15d00b2c3e358ee37bced6ba738662ec</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref48"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">48</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit48" data-doi="10.1103/PhysRevB.61.14095"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ferrari, A.                         cas_cit48R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">48</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Interpretation of Raman spectra of disordered and amorphous carbon</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ferrari, A. C.; Robertson, J.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Physical Review B: Condensed Matter and Materials Physics</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Physical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The model and theor. understanding of the Raman spectra in disordered and amorphous C are given.  The nature of the G and D vibration modes in graphite is analyzed in terms of the resonant excitation of π states and the long-range polarizability of π bonding.  Visible Raman data on disordered, amorphous, and diamondlike C are classified in a 3-stage model to show the factors that control the position, intensity, and widths of the G and D peaks.  The visible Raman spectra depend formally on the configuration of the sp2 sites in sp2-bonded clusters.  In cases where the sp2 clustering is controlled by the sp3 fraction, such as in as-deposited tetrahedral amorphous C (ta-C) or hydrogenated amorphous C (a-C:H) films, the visible Raman parameters can be used to derive the sp3 fraction.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqqdAvFvqG2KrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljCM-67xY3j3Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD3cXjs1Smu7c%253D&amp;md5=e451e6f21e1f6cf375931e6a23e836bb</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref49"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">49</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit49" data-doi="10.1063/1.2196057"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cançado, L.                         cas_cit54R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">54</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Comparative Microstructural Analysis of Nongraphitic Carbons by Wide-Angle X-ray and Neutron Scattering</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Pfaff, Torben; Badaczewski, Felix M.; Loeh, Marc O.; Franz, Alexandra; Hoffmann, Jens-Uwe; Reehuis, Manfred; Zeier, Wolfgang G.; Smarsly, Bernd M.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry C</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Non-graphitic carbons (NGCs) represent the most abundant class of sp2-hybridized carbon materials (coal, char coal, activated carbon, etc.).  These carbons consist of small graphene layer stacks possessing significant structural disorder both in the single graphene sheets and the stacking.  In this study an advanced evaluation approach for the wide-angle neutron scattering (WANS) was developed, based on the method introduced by Ruland and Smarsly (2002).  In particular, we elucidated if and how the enhanced WANS data quality and larger values of the modulus of the scattering vector <span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math" id="MJXp-Span-51"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-52">s</span></span></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-2">s</script>-range affect the accuracy and the values of the size and disorder parameters - being fitting parameters by themselves - in comparison to wide-angle x-ray scattering (WAXS), which is usually performed by lab. equipment.  We find a reasonable agreement for the parameters <span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math" id="MJXp-Span-53"><span class="MJXp-msubsup" id="MJXp-Span-54"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-55" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">L</span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-56" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-57">a</span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-3">L_\mathrm{a}</script> and <span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math" id="MJXp-Span-58"><span class="MJXp-msubsup" id="MJXp-Span-59"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-60" style="margin-right: 0.05em;">L</span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-61" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-62">c</span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-4">L_\mathrm{c}</script>, i.e. the lateral dimension and stack height, within the error bars, while for the disorder parameters different results for WAXS and WANS were found, the origin of which is discussed.  Thus, this study addresses the general issue of how reliably microstructural parameters can be detd. from WAXS/WANS, by fitting simulated WAXS and WANS curves, which are quality-impaired by added Gaussion noise at different levels and cut-off at different <span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math" id="MJXp-Span-63"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-64">s</span></span></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-5">s</script>-values.  From this anal. we estd. the minimal data quality required for a reliable NGC microstructural anal. based on WAXS/WANS.  As an important finding, these simulations show that typical, std. WAXS lab. setups are sufficient to provide reliable values for the most relevant structural parameters.  Furthermore, pair-distribution function (PDF) analyses were performed on WAXS data obtained from a Synchrotron facility.  Comparing PDF and WAXS/WANS fitting anal. thus suggests the presence of small highly ordered oligoarom. domains embedded in the larger graphene sheets, questioning the classical view on the NGC microstructure.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoOXuarm4paUrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liGBMSNE3aPfw"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit55R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">55</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Structural Analysis of Sucrose-Derived Hard Carbon and Correlation with the Electrochemical Properties for Lithium, Sodium, and Potassium Insertion</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kubota, Kei; Shimadzu, Saori; Yabuuchi, Naoaki; Tominaka, Satoshi; Shiraishi, Soshi; Abreu-Sepulveda, Maria; Manivannan, Ayyakkannu; Gotoh, Kazuma; Fukunishi, Mika; Dahbi, Mouad; Komaba, Shinichi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">32</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbon possesses the ability to store Li, Na, and K ions between stacked sp2 carbon layers and voids (micropores).  We have explored hard carbon as a candidate for neg. electrode materials for Li-ion, Na-ion, and K-ion batteries.  Hard carbon samples have been prepd. by carbonizing sucrose at different heat treatment temps. (HTTs) in the range of 700-2000°C to make them structurally suitable for reversible Li, Na, and K insertion.  Structures and particle morphol. of the hard carbon samples synthesized at different HTTs were systematically characterized using X-ray diffraction, small-angle X-ray scattering, pair distribution function anal., electron microscopy, Raman spectroscopy, and ESR spectroscopy.  All these characterizations of hard carbon samples have revealed advanced ordering of carbons and redn. of carbon defects with increasing HTT.  Thus, the av. stacked carbon interlayer distance decreases, the no. of the stacking layers increases, the layered domains grow in the in-plane direction, and interstitial voids enlarge.  Electrochem. properties of the hard carbons were examd. in nonaq. Li, Na, and K cells.  Potential profiles and reversible capacities upon galvanostatic charge/discharge processes in nonaq. cells are significantly different depending on HTTs and different alkali metal ions.  On the basis of these findings, strategies to design high-capacity hard carbon neg. electrodes for high-energy-d. Li-ion, Na-ion, and K-ion batteries are discussed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGr6G6NR3Sjg1rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liGBMSNE3aPfw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXktlOnurY%253D&amp;md5=39bd5eef10835b2e37215cb7a7f56521</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref56"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref56'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">56</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit56" data-doi="10.1080/10587250008025514"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Flandrois, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guerin, K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">On the Choice of Carbon Materials at the Negative Electrode of Li-ion Batteries: Graphite vs. Hard Carbons</span>. <i>Mol.                         cas_cit56R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">56</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">On the choice of carbon materials at the negative electrode of Li-ion batteries: graphite vs. hard carbons</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Flandrois, Serge; Guerin, Katia</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Molecular Crystals and Liquid Crystals Science and Technology, Section A: Molecular Crystals and Liquid Crystals</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">We report on the two families of carbon materials (graphites and hard carbons) known to be the most attractive host structures in Li-ion batteries.  Both present drawbacks related to the irreversible capacity.  For graphites, it is due to exfoliation, which can be suppressed by the presence of the rhombohedral form.  In fact, we show that some structure defects are responsible for this effect.  For hard carbons, a clear correlation has been found between the irreversible capacity and the closed pore vol. detd. from helium pycnometry measurements.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp9hzB9gKAtLbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liGBMSNE3aPfw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD3cXns1Sjsbc%253D&amp;md5=22e2bb685fa7b9dfcd2198712940714d</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref57"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref57'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">57</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit57" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201908209"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Olsson, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cottom, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Au, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jensen, A.                         cas_cit57R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">57</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Elucidating the Effect of Planar Graphitic Layers and Cylindrical Pores on the Storage and Diffusion of Li, Na, and K in Carbon Materials</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Olsson, Emilia; Cottom, Jonathon; Au, Heather; Guo, Zhenyu; Jensen, Anders C. S.; Alptekin, Hande; Drew, Alan J.; Titirici, Maria-Magdalena; Cai, Qiong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">30</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">17</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1908209</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AFMDC6</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbons are among the most promising materials for alkali-ion metal anodes.  These materials have a highly complex structure and understanding the metal storage and migration within these structures is of utmost importance for the development of next-generation battery technologies.  The effect of different carbon structural motifs on Li, Na, and K storage and diffusion are probed using d. functional theory based on exptl. characterizations of hard carbon samples.  Two carbon structural models-the planar graphitic layer model and the cylindrical pore model-are constructed guided by small-angle X-ray scattering and transmission electron microscopy characterization.  The planar graphitic layers with interlayer distance &lt;6.5 Å are beneficial for metal storage, but do not have significant contribution to rapid metal diffusion.  Fast diffusion is shown to take place in planar graphitic layers with interlayer distance &gt;6.5 Å, when the graphitic layer sepn. becomes so wide that there is negligible interaction between the two graphitic layers.  The cylindrical pore model, reflecting the curved morphol., does not increase metal storage, but significantly lowers the metal migration barriers.  Hence, the curved carbon morphologies are shown to have great importance for battery cycling.  These findings provide an at.-scale picture of the metal storage and diffusion in these materials.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqXHsBEictfcrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liGBMSNE3aPfw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXktVentr8%253D&amp;md5=2b8e84c76221795bf37bdb19b3349c49</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref58"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref58'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">58</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit58" data-doi="10.1021/acsenergylett.8b01761"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Anji

        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">3</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2851-2857</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AELCCP</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2380-8195</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">While the technol. importance of carbon-based anodes for sodium-ion batteries is undebated, the underlying mechanism for sodium insertion and storage is still strongly disputed.  Here, a joint exptl. and theor. study is presented that allows to provide detailed insights into the process of Na insertion in nongraphitizable (hard) carbon.  For this purpose, the data from in situ Raman scattering of Na insertion in hard carbon are combined with d. functional theory-based lattice dynamics and band structure calcns. for Na insertion in graphitic model structures used for a local description of graphitic domains in hard carbon.  The agreement of exptl. results and computational findings yields a clear picture of the Na insertion mechanism, which can be described by four different stages that are dominated by surface morphol., defect concn., bulk structure, and nanoporosity.  On the basis of the resulting model for sodium insertion, design strategies are suggested to maximize the capacity of hard carbon.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoA_kOfSPHVi7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liGBMSNE3aPfw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXhvFChsb7L&amp;md5=69d1c4978e6674e6d15b7fa167c0adc7</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref59"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref59'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">59</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit59" data-doi="10.1021/acs.chemmater.2c00405"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yuan, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Meng, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Song, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sodium Storage Mechanism of Nongraphitic Carbons: A General Model and the Function of Accessible Closed Pores</span>. <i>Chem.                         cas_cit59R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">59</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium Storage Mechanism of Nongraphitic Carbons: A General Model and the Function of Accessible Closed Pores</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yuan, Man; Cao, Bin; Liu, Huan; Meng, Chenyang; Wu, Jianping; Zhang, Su; Li, Ang; Chen, Xiaohong; Song, Huaihe</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2022</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">34</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3489-3500</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CMATEX</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Nowadays, the sodium storage mechanism of nongraphitic carbon materials still remains controversial despite their promising application as anode materials in sodium-ion batteries.  Herein, a series of hard carbons and soft carbons with different crystallinities and pore structures was prepd. from coal tar pitch, and a general mechanism is proposed through the anal. and contrast of their microstructures and electrochem. performances.  In particular, it was found that sodium adsorption and insertion take place in the form of sloping capacity for both soft carbons and hard carbons, while sodium closed pore filling only exists below 0.1 V in hard carbons corresponding to the plateau capacity.  Moreover, we further propose the concept of accessible closed pores (ACPs) and quantify the surface area of ACPs, which exhibits a high linear correlation with the plateau capacity.  This general mechanism model will provide theory instruction for the prepn. of anode materials with superior performance.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoETY7vEDdY9rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liA4lvBh_IjPA"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit60R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">60</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Low-Cost and High-Performance Hard Carbon Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Kun; Jin, Yu; Sun, Shixiong; Huang, Yangyang; Peng, Jian; Luo, Jiahuan; Zhang, Qin; Qiu, Yuegang; Fang, Chun; Han, Jiantao</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Omega</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">2</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">4</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1687-1695</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ACSODF</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">As an anode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), hard carbon (HC) presents high specific capacity and favorable cycling performance.  However, high cost and low initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of HC seriously limit its future commercialization for SIBs.  A typical biowaste, mangosteen shell was selected as a precursor to prep. low-cost and high-performance HC via a facile one-step carbonization method, and the influence of different heat treatments on the morphologies, microstructures, and electrochem. performances was investigated systematically.  The microstructure evolution studied using X-ray diffraction, Raman, Brunauer-Emmett-Teller, and high-resoln. transmission electron microscopy, along with electrochem. measurements, reveals the optimal carbonization condition of the mangosteen shell: HC carbonized at 1500 °C for 2 h delivers the highest reversible capacity of ∼330 mA h g-1 at a c.d. of 20 mA g-1, a capacity retention of ∼98% after 100 cycles, and an ICE of ∼83%.  Addnl., the sodium-ion storage behavior of HC is deeply analyzed using galvanostatic intermittent titrn. and cyclic voltammetry technologies.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp-InxsIfusHrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liA4lvBh_IjPA"> &gt;&gt;                 10.1002/adma.202304091</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D35%26publication_year%3D2023%26journal%3DAdv.%2BMater.%26author%3DZ.%2BGuo%26author%3DZ.%2BXu%26author%3DF.%2BXie%26author%3DJ.%2BJiang%26author%3DK.%2BZheng%26author%3DS.%2BAlabidun%26author%3DM.%2BCrespo-Ribadeneyra%26author%3DY.%2BHu%26author%3DH.%2BAu%26author%3DM.%2BTitirici%26title%3DInvestigating%2BThe%2BSuperior%2BPerformance%2Bof%2BHard%2BCarbon%2BAnodes%2Bin%2BSodium-Ion%2BCompared%2Bwith%2Blithium-%2Band%2Bpotassium-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1002%252Fadma.202304091&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.chemmater.3c01520&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Fadma.202304091&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit61R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">61</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Investigating the Superior Performance of Hard Carbon Anodes in Sodium-Ion Compared With Lithium- and Potassium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Guo, Zhenyu; Xu, Zhen; Xie, Fei; Jiang, Jinglin; Zheng, Kaitian; Alabidun, Sarat; Crespo-Ribadeneyra, Maria; Hu, Yong-Sheng; Au, Heather; Titirici, Maria-Magdalena</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Emerging sodium-ion batteries (NIBs) and potassium-ion batteries (KIBs) show promise in complementing lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. and diversifying the battery market.  Hard carbon is a potential anode candidate for LIBs, NIBs, and KIBs due to its high capacity, sustainability, wide availability, and stable physicochem. properties.  Herein, a series of hard carbons is synthesized by hydrothermal carbonization and subsequent pyrolysis at different temps. to finely tune their structural properties.  When tested as anodes, the hard carbons exhibit differing ion-storage trends for Li, Na, and K, with NIBs achieving the highest reversible capacity.  Extensive materials and electrochem. characterizations are carried out to study the correlation of structural features with electrochem. performance and to explain the specific mechanisms of alkali-ion storage in hard carbons.  In addn., the best-performing hard carbon is tested against a sodium cathode Na3V2(PO4)3 in a Na-ion pouch cell, displaying a high power d. of 2172 W kg-1 at an energy d. of 181.5 Wh kg-1 (based on the total wt. of active materials in both anode and cathode).  The Na-ion pouch cell also shows stable ultralong-term cycling (9000 h or 5142 cycles) and demonstrates the promising potential of such materials as sustainable, scalable anodes for beyond Li-batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpnroj9h-3qCbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liA4lvBh_IjPA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3sXhvFWmu7%252FM&amp;md5=c2746ea33f3b04b536e793346fb6aee2</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref62"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref62'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">62</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit62" data-doi="10.1039/D1MA00315A"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Thompson, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xia, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, X.                         cas_cit62R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">62</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A review on biomass-derived hard carbon materials for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Thompson, Mathew; Xia, Qingbing; Hu, Zhe; Zhao, Xiu Song</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Materials Advances</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">2</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">18</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5881-5905</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  This paper presents a review of research progress for biomass-derived hard carbon materials for sodium-ion storage.  It provides an in-depth anal. of hard carbon anode materials obtained from biomass with the aim of identifying an optimal structure performance relationship.  Different sodium-ion storage mechanisms in hard carbon and models proposed in the literature are discussed and compared.  Hard carbon property - performance correlations are analyzed based on recent published data.  This paper also provides insight into the common methods for prepg. hard carbon from biomass, as well as effect of prepn. conditions on the phys. properties of hard carbon materials.  Obsd. structural changes are directly correlated with prepn. parameters and biomass precursor used.  In most cases, biomass derived hard carbon materials thermally treated between 1200°C and 1400°C have been identified as providing ideal structural properties for improving sodium-ion storage performance in battery cells.  However, certain drawbacks including poor cycling stability and low initial coulombic efficiency still hinder the wider applications of hard carbon for sodium-ion batteries.  To that end, methods for addressing the neg. performance characteristics are provided, including the requirement for novel characterization techniques and advanced material engineering.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®"         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The template carbonization method is a powerful tool for producing carbon materials with precisely controlled structures at the nanometer level.  The resulting templated nanocarbons exhibit extraordinarily unique (often ordered) structures that could never be produced by any of the conventional methods for carbon prodn.  This review summarizes recent publications about templated nanocarbons and their composites used for energy storage applications, including hydrogen storage, electrochem. capacitors, and lithium-ion batteries.  The main objective of this review is to clarify the true significance of the templated nanocarbons for each application.  For this purpose, the performance characteristics of almost all templated nanocarbons reported thus far are listed and compared with those of conventional materials, so that the advantages/disadvantages of the templated nanocarbons are elucidated.  From the practical point of view, the high prodn. cost and poor mass-producibility of the templated nanocarbons make them rather difficult to utilize; however, the study of their unique, specific, and ordered structures enables a deeper insight into energy storage mechanisms and the guidelines for developing energy storage materials.  Thus, another important purpose of this work is to establish such general guidelines and to propose future strategies for the prodn. of carbon materials with improved performance for energy storage applications.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo8CDR-XCroM7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liKKmsAAQMqkQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XhtVehs7zE&amp;md5=90342c4e122bceee10de84abf383da96</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref3" class="rightTabRefNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit3" data-doi="10.1016/S1872-5805(22)60575-4"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hong, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xie, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gu, D.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Recent progress of mesoporous carbons applied in electrochemical catalysis</span>. <i>New Carbon Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2022</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>37</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">152</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">179</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  An ideal adsorbent should possess the ability of being easily fabricated, cheap to produce, and have a high adsorptive capacity for adsorbates.  Ordered mesoporous carbon (OMC), a moderately new member of the carbonaceous family of adsorbents, has arisen as a potential adsorbent because of its distinct features such as high BET surface area, large pore size and porosity vol., tunable surface chem. and thermo-mech. stability.  In this review paper, we have presented a systematic documentation of all the research efforts on OMC prepn., modification, characterization, and environmental application for pollutant removal.  The subsequent characterizations of OMC employing different techniques, such as, N2 Adsorption-Desorption Isotherm, scanning electron microscope (SEM) and transmission electron microscope (TEM) images, X-ray diffraction (XRD) pattern, fourier transform IR spectroscopy (FTIR) and X-ray photon spectroscopy (XPS) spectra, are summarized.  Furthermore, we have compared the adsorption performance of different modified OMCs towards different inorg. pollutants, such as heavy metals, and various org. pollutants categorically, such as dyes, phenols and their derivs., volatile org. compds. (VOCs), alkaloids, nitrogen contg. org. compds., and vitamins. De A.         </div><div class="casAbstract">This article gives an overall view of the mechanisms involved in the mesostructuring that takes place during the formation of surfactant-templated inorg. materials by evapn.  Since such a method of prepn. is well suited to fabricating thin films by dip coating, spin coating, casting, or spraying, it is of paramount interest to draw a general description of the processes occurring during the formation of self-assembled hybrid org./inorg. materials, taking into account all crit. parameters.  The following study is based on very recent works on the meso-organization of thin SiO2 films using tetraethylorthosilicate (TEOS) as the inorg. source and cetyltrimethylammonium bromide (CTAB) as the structuring agent, but the method can also be extended to other systems based on nonsilica oxides and block copolymer surfactants.  The organization depends mainly on the chem. compn. of the film when it reaches the modulable steady state (MSS), where the inorg. framework is still flexible and the compn. is stable after reaching an equil. in the diffusion of volatile species.  This MSS state is generally attained seconds after the drying line, and the film's compn. depends on various parameters: the relative vapor pressures in the environment, the evapn. conditions, and the chem. conditions in the initial soln.  Diagrams of textures, in which the stabilized structures are controlled by local min., probably are proposed to explain the complex phenomena assocd. with mesostructuring induced by evapn.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrzP5Apxes6Z7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liKKmsAAQMqkQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD2cXjvVeitrw%253D&amp;md5=fb31e7d5d67b438c7c800ad430f93a36</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref6" class="rightTabRefNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit6" data-doi="10.1021/cr0200062"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Soler-Illia, G.         </div><div class="casAbstract">C porous materials with a periodically ordered pore structure, controlled pore size and geometry and high thermal stability are synthesized using self-assembly of environmentally friendly phloroglucinol/glyoxylic acid precursors with an amphiphilic triblock copolymer template.  Glyoxylic acid, a plant-derived compd., is used for the 1st time as a substituent of carcinogen formaldehyde usually employed in such a synthesis. (up to 1 cm3 g-1), tunable pore size (0.6 nm to 7 nm) and various pore architectures (hexagonal, cubic, and ink-bottle) by tuning the precursor ratio and by applying different manufg. engineering strategies.  Insights on the synthesis mechanism of the phenolic resin and C meso-structures were obtained using several anal. techniques, i.e., NMR (13C NMR) and FTIR spectroscopy, temp. programmed desorption coupled with mass spectrometry (TPD-MS) and thermo-gravimetric anal.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Mesoporous carbons synthesized via a soft-templating approach have attracted much attention due to their easy synthesis and facile control over the derived pore structure.  In analogy to soft-templating approaches for mesoporous metal oxides, their synthesis is based on a sequence of forming supramol. arrangements of precursor mols. with the soft templates, stabilization of the precursor framework by polymn. and finally the removal of the templates.  Using micelles of amphiphilic block-copolymers as templates, facile control over the morphol. and size of mesopores can be achieved by e.g. controlling size, compn., and concn. of the template polymers or compn. and d.p. of the precursor.  Moreover, soft templating approaches can be extended to obtain also carbon materials with hierarchical meso- and macroporosity.  The addnl. macroporosity either can result from templating by polymer latex or is induced via macrophase sepn.  In this review, we describe recent progress and examples in the synthesis and application of mesoporous carbon materials based on soft-templating approaches.  Moreover, we reiterate fundamental principles of self-aggregation, highlight proposed synthesis mechanisms and present means of controlling pore size, also in hierarchical meso-macroporous carbon materials.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpo4M2vKKM7oLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lib8HU6ErZrQg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XhsV2qsLrM&amp;md5=6938a58316e10598f600e2593741470a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref9" class="rightTabRefNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit9" data-doi="10.1039/C8GC03497D"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Herou, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ribadeneyra, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Porous and nanoscale architectures of inorg. materials have become crucial for a range of energy and catalysis applications, where the ability to control the morphol. largely dets. the transport characteristics and device performance.  Despite the availability of a range of block copolymer self-assembly methods, the conditions for tuning the key architectural features such as the inorg. wall-thickness have remained elusive.  Toward this end, we have developed soln. processing guidelines that enable isomorphic nanostructures with tunable wall-thickness.  A new poly(ethylene oxide-b-hexyl acrylate) (PEO-b-PHA) structure-directing agent (SDA) was used to demonstrate the key soln. design criteria.  Specifically, the use of a polymer with a high Flory-Huggins effective interaction parameter, χ, and appropriate soln. conditions leads to the kinetic entrapment of persistent micelle templates (PMT) for tunable isomorphic architectures.  Soly. parameters are used to predict conditions for maintaining persistent micelle sizes despite changing equil. conditions.  Here, the use of different inorg. loadings controls the inorg. wall-thickness with const. pore size.  This versatile method enabled a record 55 nm oxide wall-thickness from micelle coassembly as well as the seamless transition from mesoporous materials to macroporous materials by varying the polymer molar mass and soln. conditions.  The processing guidelines are generalizable and were elaborated with three inorg. systems, including Nb2O5, WO3, and SiO2, that were thermally stable to 600 °C for access to cryst. materials.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpNanclANmqj7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljUbgP8OKbMhw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XitF2nsr8%253D&amp;md5=8bd7fda614763c13110587cce5565873</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref15" class="rightTabRefNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit15" data-doi="10.1016/j.carbon.2017.02.016"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Libbrecht, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">an Verberckmoes</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Thybaut, J.
